				<div class="minerva__subtitle"><span class="mw-redirectedfrom">&#160;&#160;(Redirected from <a href="/w/index.php?title=Citigroup_Inc.&amp;redirect=no" class="mw-redirect" title="Citigroup Inc.">Citigroup Inc.</a>)</span></div>

			<div id="bodyContent" class="content"><div id="mw-content-text" lang="en" dir="ltr" class="mw-content-ltr"><script>function mfTempOpenSection(id){var block=document.getElementById("mf-section-"+id);block.className+=" open-block";block.previousSibling.className+=" open-block";}</script><div class="mw-parser-output"><section class="mf-section-0" id="mf-section-0">
<p class="mw-empty-elt">

</p>
<p><b>Citigroup Inc.</b> or <b>Citi</b> (<a href="/wiki/Style_(visual_arts)" title="Style (visual arts)">stylized</a> as <b>citi</b>) is an American multinational <a href="/wiki/Investment_banking" title="Investment banking">investment bank</a> and <a href="/wiki/Financial_services" title="Financial services">financial services</a> corporation headquartered in <a href="/wiki/New_York_City" title="New York City">New York City</a>. The company was formed by the merger of banking giant Citicorp and financial conglomerate <a href="/wiki/Travelers_Group" class="mw-redirect" title="Travelers Group">Travelers Group</a> in 1998; Travelers was subsequently spun off from the company in 2002.<sup id="cite_ref-IHT_21-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-IHT-21">[21]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-22" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-22">[22]</a></sup> Citigroup owns Citicorp, the holding company for <a href="/wiki/Citibank" title="Citibank">Citibank</a>, as well as several international subsidiaries. Citi is incorporated in NY.<sup id="cite_ref-23" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-23">[23]</a></sup></p><table class="infobox vcard" style="width:22em"><caption class="fn org">Citigroup id="cite_ref-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-8">[8]</a></sup><br> <a href="/wiki/Citibank_(Hong_Kong)" title="Citibank (Hong Kong)">Citibank (Hong Kong)</a><sup id="cite_ref-9" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-9">[9]</a></sup><br> <a href="/wiki/Citibank_India" title="Citibank India">Citibank India</a><sup id="cite_ref-10" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-10">[10]</a></sup><br> <a href="/wiki/Citigroup_Global_Markets_Japan" title="Citigroup Global Markets Japan">Citigroup Global Markets Japan</a><sup id="cite_ref-11" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-11">[11]</a></sup><br> <a href="/wiki/Citibank_Indonesia" title="Citibank Indonesia">Citibank Indonesia</a><sup id="cite_ref-12" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-12">[12]</a></sup><br> <a href="/wiki/Citibank_Korea" title="Citibank Korea">Citibank Korea</a><sup id="cite_ref-13" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-13">[13]</a></sup> <br> <a href="/wiki/Citibank_Malaysia" class="mw-redirect" title="Citibank Malaysia">Citibank Malaysia</a><sup id="cite_ref-14" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-14">[14]</a></sup><br> <a href="/wiki/Citibank_Russia" title="Citibank Russia">Citibank Russia</a><sup id="cite_ref-15" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-15">[15]</a></sup><br> <a href="/wiki/Citibank_Singapore" title="Citibank Singapore">Citibank Singapore</a><sup id="cite_ref-16" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-16">[16]</a></sup><br> <a href="/wiki/Citibank_Uganda" title="Citibank Uganda">Citibank Uganda</a><sup id="cite_ref-17" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-17">[17]</a></sup><br> <a href="/wiki/Citibank_United_Arab_Emirates" title="Citibank United Arab Emirates">Citibank United Arab Emirates</a><sup id="cite_ref-18" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-18">[18]</a></sup><br> <a href="/wiki/Grupo_Financiero_Banamex" title="Grupo Financiero Banamex">Grupo Financiero Banamex</a><sup id="cite_ref-19" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-19">[19]</a></sup></td></tr><tr><th scope="row" style="padding-right: 0.5em;"><a href="/wiki/Basel_III" title="Basel III">Capital ratio</a></th><td style="line-height: 1.35em;">12.4% <span style="font-size:85%;">(2017)</span></td></tr><tr><th scope="row" style="padding-right: 0.5em;">Website</th><td style="line-height: 1.35em;"><span class="url"><a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="http://www.citigroup.com/">www<wbr></wbr>.citigroup<wbr></wbr>.com</a></span></td></tr><tr><td colspan="2" style="text-align:center;line-height: 1.35em;"><b>Footnotes / references</b><br><sup id="cite_ref-10K_20-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-10K-20">[20]</a></sup></td></tr></tbody></table><p>Citigroup is ranked 3rd on the <a href="/wiki/List_of_largest_banks_in_the_United_States" title="List of largest banks in the United States">list of largest banks in the United States</a> and, alongside <a href="/wiki/JPMorgan_Chase" title="JPMorgan Chase">JPMorgan Chase</a>, <a href="/wiki/Bank_of_America" title="Bank of America">Bank of America</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Wells_Fargo" title="Wells Fargo">Wells Fargo</a>, it is one of the <a href="/wiki/Big_Four_(banking)#United_States" title="Big Four (banking)">Big Four</a> banks of the United States.<sup id="cite_ref-24" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-24">[24]</a></sup> It is a <a href="/wiki/Systemically_important_financial_institution" title="Systemically important financial institution">systemically important financial institution</a> and is on the <a href="/wiki/List_of_systemically_important_banks" title="List of systemically important banks">list of systemically important banks</a> that are commonly cited as being <a href="/wiki/Too_big_to_fail" title="Too big to fail">too big to fail</a>. It is one of the nine global investment banks in the <a href="/wiki/Bulge_Bracket" title="Bulge Bracket">Bulge Bracket</a>.
</p><p>Citigroup is ranked 30th on the <a href="/wiki/Fortune_500" title="Fortune 500">Fortune 500</a> as of 2019.<sup id="cite_ref-25" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-25">[25]</a></sup> Citigroup has over 200 million customer accounts and does business in more than 160 countries.<sup id="cite_ref-10K_20-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-10K-20">[20]</a></sup> It has 214,000 employees,<sup id="cite_ref-10K_20-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-10K-20">[20]</a></sup> although it had 357,000 employees before the <a href="/wiki/Financial_crisis_of_2007-2008" class="mw-redirect" title="Financial crisis of 2007-2008">financial crisis of 2007-2008</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-26" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-26">[26]</a></sup> when it was rescued via a massive stimulus package by the U.S. government.<sup id="cite_ref-27" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-27">[27]</a></sup></p>

</section><h2 class="section-heading" onclick="javascript:mfTempOpenSection(1)"><div class="mw-ui-icon mw-ui-icon-element indicator mw-ui-icon-small mw-ui-icon-flush-left"></div><span class="mw-headline" id="Current_operations">Current operations</span><span class="mw-editsection"><a href="/w/index.php?title=Citigroup&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Current operations" data-section="1" class="mw-ui-icon mw-ui-icon-element mw-ui-icon-wikimedia-edit-base20 edit-page mw-ui-icon-flush-right">Edit</a></span></h2><section class="mf-section-1 collapsible-block" id="mf-section-1">
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Citi_Private_Bank_Office.jpg" class="image"><noscript><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/d/df/Citi_Private_Bank_Office.jpg/220px-Citi_Private_Bank_Office.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="368" class="thumbimage" data-file-width="1952" data-file-height="3264"></noscript><span class="lazy-image-placeholder" style="width: 220px;height: 368px;" data-src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/d/df/Citi_Private_Bank_Office.jpg/220px-Citi_Private_Bank_Office.jpg" data-alt="" data-width="220" data-height="368" data-class="thumbimage"> </span></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Citi_Private_Bank_Office.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A Citi Private Bank Office</div></div></div>
<p>Citigroup is the holding company for the following divisions:<sup id="cite_ref-28" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-28">[28]</a></sup></p>
<ul><li>Citigroup Global Markets, Inc., Citigroup Global Markets Limited (UK), and <a href="/wiki/Citigroup_Global_Markets_Japan" title="Citigroup Global Markets Japan">Citigroup Global Markets Japan</a> - broker dealers, including one of 24 <a href="/wiki/Primary_dealer" title="Primary dealer">primary dealers</a> in <a href="/wiki/United_States_Treasury" class="mw-redirect" title="United States Treasury">United States Treasury</a> securities.<sup id="cite_ref-29" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-29">[29]</a></sup><ul><li>Citi's Institutional Clients Group (ICG) offers investment and corporate banking services and products for companies, governments, institutions, and ultra high-net-worth investors. ICG consists of the following five main divisions:<sup id="cite_ref-30" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-30">[30]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-31" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-31">[31]</a></sup><ul><li><b>Capital Markets Origination</b> is focused on the capital-raising needs such as public offerings, private placements, and <a href="/wiki/Special-purpose_acquisition_company" title="Special-purpose acquisition company">special purpose acquisition companies</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-32" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-32">[32]</a></sup></li>
<li><b>Corporate &amp; Investment Banking</b> provides strategic and financing products and advisory services to multinational and local corporations, financial institutions, governments, and privately held businesses in more than 160 countries. It also provides client services such as mergers &amp; acquisitions advice and underwriting of initial public offerings.<sup id="cite_ref-33" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-33">[33]</a></sup></li>
<li><b>Markets &amp; Securities Services</b> includes investor services and direct custody and clearing, <a href="/wiki/Hedge_fund" title="Hedge fund">hedge fund</a> and <a href="/wiki/Private_equity" title="Private equity">private equity</a> servicing, and issuer businesses. It provides financial products through underwriting, sales &amp; trading of a range of investment assets. Products offered include servicing of equities, commodities, credit, futures, <a href="/wiki/Foreign_exchange_market" title="Foreign exchange market">foreign exchange</a> (FX), emerging markets, G10 rates, municipals, prime finance/brokerage services, and securitized markets, such as <a href="/wiki/Collateralized_debt_obligation" title="Collateralized debt obligation">collateralized debt obligations</a> and <a href="/wiki/Mortgage-backed_security" title="Mortgage-backed security">mortgage-backed securities</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-34" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-34">[34]</a></sup> Its Citi Research team provides equity and fixed income research, company, sector, economic and geographic market analysis, and product-specific analysis for Citi's individual and institutional clients. Its flagship research reports include the following: Portfolio Strategist, Bond Market Roundup, U.S. Economics Weekly, International Market Roundup, Global Economic Outlook &amp; Strategy and the Global Equity Strategist.<sup id="cite_ref-35" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-35">[35]</a></sup></li>
<li><b><a href="/wiki/Citi_Private_Bank" title="Citi Private Bank">Citi Private Bank</a></b> advises professional investors, <a href="/wiki/High-net-worth_individual#Ultra_high-net-worth_individuals" title="High-net-worth individual">ultra high-net-worth individuals</a> and families, and lawyers throughout the world. It uses an open architecture network of more than 800 private bankers and investment professionals across 46 countries and jurisdictions to provide clients access to global investment opportunities. It has over $250 billion in assets under management. The minimum net worth requirement is $25 million in liquid assets and is waived for only law firm groups and other clients under special circumstances.<sup id="cite_ref-36" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-36">[36]</a></sup></li>
<li><b>Treasury and Trade Solutions (TTS)</b><sup id="cite_ref-37" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-37">[37]</a></sup> provides cash management, trade and securities services to companies, governments, and other institutions in the U.S. and more than 140 countries. TTS intermediates more than $3 trillion in global transactions daily. It has over $13 trillion assets under custody, about $377 billion in average liability balances, serves 99% of world's Fortune 100 companies and ~85% of the world's Fortune 500 companies, and has 10 regional processing centers worldwide using global processes. Institutions use TTS to support their treasury operations with global solutions<sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Use_plain_English#Buzzwords" title="Wikipedia:Use plain English"><span title="The material near this tag may use buzzwords designed solely to impress you or obscure meaning. (November 2018)">buzzword</span></a></i>]</sup> for payments, collections, liquidity and investments by working in partnership with export credit agencies and development banks. It also sells supply chain financing products as well as medium- and long-term global financing programs across multiple industries. Clients doing business with Citi in 10 or more countries generate more than 60% of Transaction Services' total revenues.</li></ul></li></ul></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Citibanamex" class="mw-redirect" title="Citibanamex">Citibanamex</a> - the second largest bank in <a href="/wiki/Mexico" title="Mexico">Mexico</a>, purchased by Citigroup in 2001; it serves about 20 million clients.</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Citicorp" class="mw-redirect" title="Citicorp">Citicorp</a> - the holding company for <a href="/wiki/Citibank" title="Citibank">Citibank</a> as well as several international banks. Citicorp contains two core businesses, Global Consumer Banking<sup id="cite_ref-38" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-38">[38]</a></sup> and Institutional Clients Group.<sup id="cite_ref-39" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-39">[39]</a></sup><ul><li><b>Citibank Retail banking</b> encompasses Citi's global branch network, branded <a href="/wiki/Citibank" title="Citibank">Citibank</a>. Citibank has more than 4,600 branches in the world and holds more than $300 billion in deposits. Citibank is the 4th largest retail bank in the United States based on deposits, and it has Citibank branded branches in countries throughout the world, with the exception of Mexico which is under a separate subsidiary called Banamex. Citibank offers checking and savings accounts, small business and commercial banking and personal wealth management among its services. Citibank offers <a href="/wiki/Citigold" class="mw-redirect" title="Citigold">Citigold</a> services worldwide to <a href="/wiki/Mass_affluent" title="Mass affluent">mass affluent</a> clients with at least US$200,000 in liquid assets. In certain markets, Citigold Select is available for clients with at least US$500,000 in liquid assets.<sup id="cite_ref-40" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-40">[40]</a></sup> Its highest level of service, Citigold Private Client, is for <a href="/wiki/High-net-worth_individual" title="High-net-worth individual">high-net-worth individuals</a> with at least $1–$3 million in liquid assets (depending on the market region) and offers access to investments and ideas from <a href="/wiki/Citi_Private_Bank" title="Citi Private Bank">Citi Private Bank</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-41" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-41">[41]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-42" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-42">[42]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-43" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-43">[43]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-44" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-44">[44]</a></sup></li>
<li><b>Citi Branded Cards</b> is the world's largest credit card issuer.<sup id="cite_ref-45" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-45">[45]</a></sup></li>
<li><b>Citi Retail Services</b> is one of the largest providers of private label and co-branded credit cards, retail consulting services, and retail loyalty products in the U.S.<sup id="cite_ref-46" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-46">[46]</a></sup></li>
<li><b>Citi Commercial Bank</b> serves 100,000 small to medium-size companies in 32 countries.<sup id="cite_ref-47" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-47">[47]</a></sup></li>
<li><b>CitiMortgage</b> originates real estate mortgages.<sup id="cite_ref-48" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-48">[48]</a></sup></li></ul></li></ul></section><h2 class="section-heading" onclick="javascript:mfTempOpenSection(2)"><div class="mw-ui-icon mw-ui-icon-element indicator mw-ui-icon-small mw-ui-icon-flush-left"></div><span class="mw-headline" id="History">History</span><span class="mw-editsection"><a href="/w/index.php?title=Citigroup&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: History" data-section="2" class="mw-ui-icon mw-ui-icon-element mw-ui-icon-wikimedia-edit-base20 edit-page mw-ui-icon-flush-right">Edit</a></span></h2><section class="mf-section-2 collapsible-block" id="mf-section-2">
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:202px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Citi.svg" class="image"><noscript><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1b/Citi.svg/200px-Citi.svg.png" decoding="async" width="200" height="130" class="thumbimage" data-file-width="300" data-file-height="195"></noscript><span class="lazy-image-placeholder" style="width: 200px;height: 130px;" data-src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1b/Citi.svg/200px-Citi.svg.png" data-alt="" data-width="200" data-height="130" data-srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1b/Citi.svg/300px-Citi.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1b/Citi.svg/400px-Citi.svg.png 2x" data-class="thumbimage"> </span></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Citi.svg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The Citigroup logo, 2000–present</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:202px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Citigroup.svg" class="image"><noscript><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f8/Citigroup.svg/200px-Citigroup.svg.png" decoding="async" width="200" height="121" class="thumbimage" data-file-width="250" data-file-height="151"></noscript><span class="lazy-image-placeholder" style="width: 200px;height: 121px;" data-src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f8/Citigroup.svg/200px-Citigroup.svg.png" data-alt="" data-width="200" data-height="121" data-srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f8/Citigroup.svg/300px-Citigroup.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f8/Citigroup.svg/400px-Citigroup.svg.png 2x" data-class="thumbimage"> </span></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Citigroup.svg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The Citigroup logo, 2007–2011</div></div></div>
<p>Citigroup was formed on October 8, 1998, following the $140 billion merger of Citicorp and <a href="/wiki/Travelers_Group" class="mw-redirect" title="Travelers Group">Travelers Group</a> to create the world's largest financial services organization.<sup id="cite_ref-IHT_21-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-IHT-21">[21]</a></sup> The history of the company is, thus, divided into the workings of several firms that over time amalgamated into Citicorp, a multinational banking corporation operating in more than 100 countries; or Travelers Group, whose businesses covered credit services, consumer finance, brokerage, and insurance. As such, the company history dates back to the founding of: the City Bank of New York (later <a href="/wiki/Citibank" title="Citibank">Citibank</a>) in 1812; Bank Handlowy in 1870; <a href="/wiki/Smith_Barney" class="mw-redirect" title="Smith Barney">Smith Barney</a> in 1873, <a href="/wiki/Banamex" class="mw-redirect" title="Banamex">Banamex</a> in 1884; <a href="/wiki/Salomon_Brothers" title="Salomon Brothers">Salomon Brothers</a> in 1910.<sup id="cite_ref-citihistory_49-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-citihistory-49">[49]</a></sup></p>
<h3 class="in-block"><span id="Citicorp_.281812.E2.80.931985.29"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Citicorp_(1812–1985)"><span id="Citicorp"></span>Citicorp (1812–1985)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><a href="/w/index.php?title=Citigroup&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Citicorp (1812–1985)" data-section="3" class="mw-ui-icon mw-ui-icon-element mw-ui-icon-wikimedia-edit-base20 edit-page mw-ui-icon-flush-right">Edit</a></span></h3>
<p><a href="/wiki/City_Bank_of_New_York" class="mw-redirect" title="City Bank of New York">City Bank of New York</a> was chartered by <a href="/wiki/New_York_State" class="mw-redirect" title="New York State">New York State</a> on June 16, 1812, with $2 million of capital. Serving a group of New York <a href="/wiki/Merchant" title="Merchant">merchants</a>, the bank opened for business on September 14 of that year, and <a href="/wiki/Samuel_Osgood" title="Samuel Osgood">Samuel Osgood</a> was elected as the first President of the company.<sup id="cite_ref-citihistory_49-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-citihistory-49">[49]</a></sup> The company's name was changed to The National City Bank of New York in 1865 after it joined the new U.S. national banking system, and it became the largest American bank by 1895.<sup id="cite_ref-citihistory_49-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-citihistory-49">[49]</a></sup> It became the first contributor to the <a href="/wiki/Federal_Reserve_Bank_of_New_York" title="Federal Reserve Bank of New York">Federal Reserve Bank of New York</a> in 1913, and the following year it inaugurated the first overseas branch of a U.S. bank in <a href="/wiki/Buenos_Aires" title="Buenos Aires">Buenos Aires</a>, although the bank had, since the mid-19th century, been active in plantation economies, such as the Cuban sugar industry. The 1918 purchase of U.S. overseas bank International Banking Corporation helped it become the first American bank to surpass $1 billion in assets, and it became the largest commercial bank in the world in 1929.<sup id="cite_ref-citihistory_49-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-citihistory-49">[49]</a></sup> As it grew, the bank became a leading innovator in financial services, becoming the first major U.S. bank to offer <a href="/wiki/Compound_interest" title="Compound interest">compound interest</a> on <a href="/wiki/Saving" title="Saving">savings</a> (1921); <a href="/wiki/Unsecured_loan" class="mw-redirect" title="Unsecured loan">unsecured personal loans</a> (1928); customer <a href="/wiki/Cheque" title="Cheque">checking accounts</a> (1936) and the negotiable <a href="/wiki/Certificate_of_deposit" title="Certificate of deposit">certificate of deposit</a> (1961).<sup id="cite_ref-citihistory_49-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-citihistory-49">[49]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-wsj20121017_50-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-wsj20121017-50">[50]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-yahoopandit_51-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-yahoopandit-51">[51]</a></sup></p><p>The bank changed its name to The First National City Bank of New York in 1955, which was shortened in 1962 to First National City Bank on the 150th anniversary of the company's foundation.<sup id="cite_ref-citihistory_49-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-citihistory-49">[49]</a></sup> The company organically entered the leasing and credit card sectors, and its introduction of U.S. dollar–denominated certificates of deposit in <a href="/wiki/London" title="London">London</a> marked the first new negotiable instrument in market since 1888. The bank introduced its First National City Charge Service credit card—popularly known as the "<a href="/wiki/Everything_card" class="mw-redirect" title="Everything card">Everything card</a>" and later to become <a href="/wiki/MasterCard" class="mw-redirect" title="MasterCard">MasterCard</a>—in 1967.<sup id="cite_ref-citihistory_49-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-citihistory-49">[49]</a></sup> Also in 1967, First National City Bank reorganized as a one-bank holding company, First National City Corporation, or "Citicorp" for short. The bank had been nicknamed "Citibank" since the 1860s, when it began using this as an eight-letter wire code address.<sup id="cite_ref-RefBiz_52-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-RefBiz-52">[52]</a></sup></p><p>In 1974, under the leadership of CEO <a href="/wiki/Walter_B._Wriston" class="mw-redirect" title="Walter B. Wriston">Walter B. Wriston</a>, First National City Corporation changed its formal name to "Citicorp," with First National City Bank being formally renamed Citibank in 1976.<sup id="cite_ref-RefBiz_52-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-RefBiz-52">[52]</a></sup> Shortly afterward, the bank launched the Citicard, which pioneered the use of 24-hour <a href="/wiki/Automated_teller_machine" title="Automated teller machine">ATMs</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-citihistory_49-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-citihistory-49">[49]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/John_S._Reed" title="John S. Reed">John S. Reed</a> was elected CEO in 1984, and Citi became a founding member of the <a href="/wiki/CHAPS" title="CHAPS">CHAPS</a> clearing house in London. Under his leadership, the next 14 years would see Citibank become the largest bank in the United States and the largest issuer of credit cards and charge cards in the world, and expand its global reach to over 90 countries.<sup id="cite_ref-citihistory_49-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-citihistory-49">[49]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-wsj20121017_50-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-wsj20121017-50">[50]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-yahoopandit_51-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-yahoopandit-51">[51]</a></sup></p>
<h3 class="in-block"><span id="Travelers_Group_.281986.E2.80.932007.29"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Travelers_Group_(1986–2007)">Travelers Group (1986–2007)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><a href="/w/index.php?title=Citigroup&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Travelers Group (1986–2007)" data-section="4" class="mw-ui-icon mw-ui-icon-element mw-ui-icon-wikimedia-edit-base20 edit-page mw-ui-icon-flush-right">Edit</a></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:154px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Travelers_logo.png" class="image"><noscript><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/3/3f/Travelers_logo.png" decoding="async" width="152" height="46" class="thumbimage" data-file-width="152" data-file-height="46"></noscript><span class="lazy-image-placeholder" style="width: 152px;height: 46px;" data-src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/3/3f/Travelers_logo.png" data-alt="" data-width="152" data-height="46" data-class="thumbimage"> </span></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Travelers_logo.png" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The corporate logo of Travelers Inc. (1993–1998) prior to merger with Citicorp.</div></div></div>
<p>Travelers Group, at the time of merger, was a diverse group of financial concerns that had been brought together under CEO <a href="/wiki/Sandy_Weill" class="mw-redirect" title="Sandy Weill">Sandy Weill</a>. Its roots came from Commercial Credit, a subsidiary of <a href="/wiki/Control_Data_Corporation" title="Control Data Corporation">Control Data Corporation</a> that was taken private by Weill in November 1986 after taking charge of the company earlier that year.<sup id="cite_ref-IHT_21-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-IHT-21">[21]</a></sup> Two years later, Weill mastered the buyout of <a href="/wiki/Primerica_Financial_Services" class="mw-redirect" title="Primerica Financial Services">Primerica Financial Services</a>—a conglomerate that had already bought <a href="/wiki/Life_insurance" title="Life insurance">life insurance</a> company A L Williams as well as <a href="/wiki/Brokerage_firm" class="mw-redirect" title="Brokerage firm">brokerage firm</a> <a href="/wiki/Smith_Barney" class="mw-redirect" title="Smith Barney">Smith Barney</a>. The new company took the Primerica name, and employed a "<a href="/wiki/Cross-selling" title="Cross-selling">cross-selling</a>" strategy such that each of the entities within the parent company aimed to sell each other's services. Its non-financial businesses were <a href="/wiki/Corporate_spin-off" title="Corporate spin-off">spun off</a>.
</p><p>In September 1992, <a href="/wiki/Travelers_Insurance" class="mw-redirect" title="Travelers Insurance">Travelers Insurance</a>, which had suffered from poor real estate investments<sup id="cite_ref-IHT_21-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-IHT-21">[21]</a></sup> and sustained significant losses in the aftermath of <a href="/wiki/Hurricane_Andrew" title="Hurricane Andrew">Hurricane Andrew</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-53" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-53">[53]</a></sup> formed a strategic alliance with Primerica that would lead to its amalgamation into a single company in December 1993. With the acquisition, the group became Travelers Inc. Property &amp; casualty and life &amp; annuities <a href="/wiki/Underwriting" title="Underwriting">underwriting</a> capabilities were added to the business. Meanwhile, the distinctive Travelers red umbrella logo, which was also acquired in the deal, was applied to all the businesses within the newly named organization. During this period, Travelers acquired <a href="/wiki/Shearson_Lehman" class="mw-redirect" title="Shearson Lehman">Shearson Lehman</a>—a retail brokerage and asset management firm that was headed by Weill until 1985<sup id="cite_ref-IHT_21-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-IHT-21">[21]</a></sup>—and merged it with Smith Barney.
</p>
<h4 class="in-block"><span id="Ownership_of_Salomon_Brothers_.281997.E2.80.932003.29"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Ownership_of_Salomon_Brothers_(1997–2003)">Ownership of Salomon Brothers (1997–2003)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><a href="/w/index.php?title=Citigroup&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Ownership of Salomon Brothers (1997–2003)" data-section="5" class="mw-ui-icon mw-ui-icon-element mw-ui-icon-wikimedia-edit-base20 edit-page mw-ui-icon-flush-right">Edit</a></span></h4>
<p>In November 1997, Travelers Group (which had been renamed again in April 1995 when they merged with Aetna Property and Casualty, Inc.), acquired <a href="/wiki/Salomon_Brothers" title="Salomon Brothers">Salomon Brothers</a>, a major <a href="/wiki/Bond_market" title="Bond market">bond dealer</a> and <a href="/wiki/Bulge_bracket" class="mw-redirect" title="Bulge bracket">bulge bracket</a> <a href="/wiki/Investment_bank" class="mw-redirect" title="Investment bank">investment bank</a>, in a $9 billion transaction.<sup id="cite_ref-54" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-54">[54]</a></sup> This deal complemented Travelers/<a href="/wiki/Smith_Barney" class="mw-redirect" title="Smith Barney">Smith Barney</a> well as Salomon was focused on fixed-income and institutional clients, whereas Smith Barney was strong in equities and retail. Salomon Brothers absorbed Smith Barney into the new securities unit termed Salomon Smith Barney; a year later, the division incorporated Citicorp's former securities operations as well. The Salomon Smith Barney name was abandoned in October 2003 after a series of financial scandals that tarnished the bank's reputation.<sup id="cite_ref-55" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-55">[55]</a></sup></p>
<h3 class="in-block"><span id="Merger_of_Citicorp_and_Travelers_.281998.E2.80.932001.29"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Merger_of_Citicorp_and_Travelers_(1998–2001)">Merger of Citicorp and Travelers (1998–2001)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><a href="/w/index.php?title=Citigroup&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Merger of Citicorp and Travelers (1998–2001)" data-section="6" class="mw-ui-icon mw-ui-icon-element mw-ui-icon-wikimedia-edit-base20 edit-page mw-ui-icon-flush-right">Edit</a></span></h3>
<p>On April 6, 1998, the merger between Citicorp and Travelers Group was announced to the world, creating a $140 billion firm with assets of almost $700 billion.<sup id="cite_ref-IHT_21-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-IHT-21">[21]</a></sup> The deal would enable Travelers to market mutual funds and insurance to Citicorp's retail customers while giving the banking divisions access to an expanded client base of investors and insurance buyers.
</p><p>In the transaction, Travelers Group acquired all Citicorp shares for $70 billion in stock, issuing 2.5 new Citigroup shares for each Citicorp share. Existing shareholders of each company owned about half of the new firm.<sup id="cite_ref-IHT_21-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-IHT-21">[21]</a></sup> While the new company maintained Citicorp's "Citi" brand in its name, it adopted Travelers' distinctive "red umbrella" as the new corporate logo, which was used until 2007.<sup id="cite_ref-56" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-56">[56]</a></sup></p><p>The chairmen of both parent companies, <a href="/wiki/John_S._Reed" title="John S. Reed">John S. Reed</a> and <a href="/wiki/Sandy_Weill" class="mw-redirect" title="Sandy Weill">Sandy Weill</a> respectively, were announced as co-chairmen and co-CEOs of the new company, Citigroup, Inc., although the vast difference in management styles between the two immediately presented question marks over the wisdom of such a setup.
</p><p>The remaining provisions of the <a href="/wiki/Glass%E2%80%93Steagall_Act" class="mw-redirect" title="Glass–Steagall Act">Glass–Steagall Act</a>—enacted following the Great Depression—forbade banks to merge with insurance underwriters, and meant Citigroup had between two and five years to divest any prohibited assets. However, Weill stated at the time of the merger that they believed "that over that time the legislation will change...we have had enough discussions to believe this will not be a problem".<sup id="cite_ref-IHT_21-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-IHT-21">[21]</a></sup> Indeed, the passing of the <a href="/wiki/Gramm-Leach-Bliley_Act" class="mw-redirect" title="Gramm-Leach-Bliley Act">Gramm-Leach-Bliley Act</a> in November 1999 vindicated Reed and Weill's views, opening the door to financial services conglomerates offering a mix of commercial banking, investment banking, insurance underwriting and brokerage.<sup id="cite_ref-57" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-57">[57]</a></sup></p><p><a href="/wiki/Joe_J._Plumeri" title="Joe J. Plumeri">Joe J. Plumeri</a> worked on the post-merger integration of the two companies and was appointed CEO of Citibank North America by Weill and Reed. He oversaw its network of 450 <a href="/wiki/Branch_(banking)" title="Branch (banking)">branches</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-plumeriwsj_58-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-plumeriwsj-58">[58]</a></sup> J. Paul Newsome, an analyst with <a href="/wiki/CIBC_Oppenheimer" class="mw-redirect" title="CIBC Oppenheimer">CIBC Oppenheimer</a>, said: "He's not the spit-and-polish executive many people expected. He's rough on the edges. But Citibank knows the bank as an institution is in trouble—it can't get away anymore with passive selling—and Plumeri has all the passion to throw a glass of cold water on the bank."<sup id="cite_ref-59" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-59">[59]</a></sup> Plumeri boosted the unit's earnings from $108 million to $415 million in one year, an increase of nearly 300%.<sup id="cite_ref-60" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-60">[60]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-61" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-61">[61]</a></sup> He unexpectedly retired from Citibank, however, in January 2000.<sup id="cite_ref-plumeriwsj_58-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-plumeriwsj-58">[58]</a></sup></p><p><span id="Associates_First_Capital_Corporation"></span><span id="Associates_Corporation_of_North_America"></span><span id="Associates_National_Bank"></span><span id="The_Associates"></span>
In 2000, Citigroup acquired Associates First Capital Corporation for  $31.1 billion in stock,<sup id="cite_ref-62" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-62">[62]</a></sup> which, until 1989, had been owned by <a href="/wiki/Gulf%2BWestern" class="mw-redirect" title="Gulf+Western">Gulf+Western</a> (now part of <a href="/wiki/National_Amusements" title="National Amusements">National Amusements</a>),<sup id="cite_ref-63" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-63">[63]</a></sup> and later by <a href="/wiki/Ford_Motor_Credit_Company" title="Ford Motor Credit Company">Ford Motor Credit Company</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-ct-1989aug13_64-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ct-1989aug13-64">[64]</a></sup> The Associates was widely criticized for predatory lending practices and Citi eventually settled with the Federal Trade Commission by agreeing to pay $240 million to customers who had been victims of a variety of predatory practices, including "flipping" mortgages, "packing" mortgages with optional credit insurance, and deceptive marketing practices.<sup id="cite_ref-Citigroup-Oct-2002-8-K_65-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Citigroup-Oct-2002-8-K-65">[65]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-66" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-66">[66]</a></sup></p><p>In 2001, Citigroup made additional acquisitions: <a href="/wiki/European_American_Bank" class="mw-redirect" title="European American Bank">European American Bank</a>, in July, for $1.9 billion,<sup id="cite_ref-67" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-67">[67]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-68" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-68">[68]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-69" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-69">[69]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-70" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-70">[70]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-71" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-71">[71]</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Banamex" class="mw-redirect" title="Banamex">Banamex</a> in August, for $12.5 billion.<sup id="cite_ref-72" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-72">[72]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-73" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-73">[73]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-74" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-74">[74]</a></sup></p>
<h3 class="in-block"><span id="Spin-off_of_Travelers_.282002.29"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Spin-off_of_Travelers_(2002)">Spin-off of Travelers (2002)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><a href="/w/index.php?title=Citigroup&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Spin-off of Travelers (2002)" data-section="7" class="mw-ui-icon mw-ui-icon-element mw-ui-icon-wikimedia-edit-base20 edit-page mw-ui-icon-flush-right">Edit</a></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:The_Travelers_Companies.svg" class="image"><noscript><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/9/9e/The_Travelers_Companies.svg/220px-The_Travelers_Companies.svg.png" decoding="async" width="220" height="51" class="thumbimage" data-file-width="250" data-file-height="58"></noscript><span class="lazy-image-placeholder" style="width: 220px;height: 51px;" data-src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/9/9e/The_Travelers_Companies.svg/220px-The_Travelers_Companies.svg.png" data-alt="" data-width="220" data-height="51" data-srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/9/9e/The_Travelers_Companies.svg/330px-The_Travelers_Companies.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/9/9e/The_Travelers_Companies.svg/440px-The_Travelers_Companies.svg.png 2x" data-class="thumbimage"> </span></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:The_Travelers_Companies.svg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The current logo for Travelers Companies</div></div></div>
<p>The company spun off its Travelers Property and Casualty insurance underwriting business in 2002.<sup id="cite_ref-75" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-75">[75]</a></sup> The spin off was prompted by the insurance unit's drag on Citigroup stock price because Travelers earnings were more seasonal and vulnerable to large disasters and events such as the <a href="/wiki/September_11_attacks" title="September 11 attacks">September 11 attacks</a>. It was also difficult to sell insurance directly to its customers since most customers were accustomed to purchasing insurance through a broker.<sup id="cite_ref-76" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-76">[76]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-77" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-77">[77]</a></sup></p><p>Travelers merged with The St. Paul Companies Inc. in 2004 forming The St. Paul Travelers Companies.<sup id="cite_ref-78" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-78">[78]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-79" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-79">[79]</a></sup> Citigroup retained the life insurance and annuities underwriting business; however, it sold those businesses to <a href="/wiki/MetLife" title="MetLife">MetLife</a> in 2005.<sup id="cite_ref-80" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-80">[80]</a></sup> Citigroup still sells life insurance through Citibank, but it no longer underwrites insurance.<sup id="cite_ref-81" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-81">[81]</a></sup></p><p>In spite of divesting Travelers Insurance, Citigroup retained Travelers' signature red umbrella logo as its own until February 2007, when Citigroup agreed to sell the logo back to St. Paul Travelers,<sup id="cite_ref-82" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-82">[82]</a></sup> which renamed itself <a href="/wiki/Travelers_Companies" class="mw-redirect" title="Travelers Companies">Travelers Companies</a>. Citigroup also decided to adopt the corporate brand "Citi" for itself and virtually all its subsidiaries, except Primerica and Banamex.
</p>
<h3 class="in-block"><span id="Subprime_mortgage_crisis_.282007.29"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Subprime_mortgage_crisis_(2007)">Subprime mortgage crisis (2007)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><a href="/w/index.php?title=Citigroup&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Subprime mortgage crisis (2007)" data-section="8" class="mw-ui-icon mw-ui-icon-element mw-ui-icon-wikimedia-edit-base20 edit-page mw-ui-icon-flush-right">Edit</a></span></h3>
<p>Heavy exposure to troubled mortgages in the form of <a href="/wiki/Collateralized_debt_obligation" title="Collateralized debt obligation">collateralized debt obligation</a> (CDOs), compounded by poor risk management, led Citigroup into trouble as the <a href="/wiki/Subprime_mortgage_crisis" title="Subprime mortgage crisis">subprime mortgage crisis</a> worsened in 2007. The company had used elaborate mathematical risk models which looked at mortgages in particular geographical areas, but never included the possibility of a national housing downturn, or the prospect that millions of mortgage holders would default on their mortgages. Trading head <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Maheras" title="Thomas Maheras">Thomas Maheras</a> was close friends with senior risk officer David Bushnell, which undermined risk oversight.<sup id="cite_ref-83" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-83">[83]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-84" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-84">[84]</a></sup> As Treasury Secretary, <a href="/wiki/Robert_Rubin" title="Robert Rubin">Robert Rubin</a> was said to be influential in lifting the <a href="/wiki/Glass%E2%80%93Steagall_Act" class="mw-redirect" title="Glass–Steagall Act">Glass–Steagall Act</a> that allowed Travelers and Citicorp to merge in 1998. Then on the board of directors of Citigroup, Rubin and Charles Prince were said to be influential in pushing the company towards <a href="/wiki/Mortgage-backed_security" title="Mortgage-backed security">MBS</a> and CDOs in the subprime mortgage market.
</p><p>Starting in June 2006, Senior Vice President <a href="/wiki/Richard_M._Bowen_III" title="Richard M. Bowen III">Richard M. Bowen III</a>, the chief underwriter of Citigroup's Consumer Lending Group, began warning the board of directors about the extreme risks being taken on by the mortgage operation that could potentially result in massive losses. The group bought and sold $90 billion of residential mortgages annually. Bowen's responsibility was essentially to serve as the quality control supervisor ensuring the unit's creditworthiness. When Bowen first became a <a href="/wiki/Whistleblower" title="Whistleblower">whistleblower</a> in 2006, 60% of the mortgages were defective. The amount of bad mortgages began increasing throughout 2007 and eventually exceeded 80% of the volume. Many of the mortgages were not only defective, but were a result of <a href="/wiki/Mortgage_fraud" title="Mortgage fraud">mortgage fraud</a>. Bowen attempted to rouse the board via weekly reports and other communications. On November 3, 2007, Bowen emailed Citigroup Chairman <a href="/wiki/Robert_Rubin" title="Robert Rubin">Robert Rubin</a> and the bank's <a href="/wiki/Chief_financial_officer" title="Chief financial officer">chief financial officer</a>, head auditor and the chief risk management officer to again expose the risk and potential losses, claiming that the group's internal controls had broken down and requesting an outside investigation of his business unit. The subsequent investigation revealed that at the Consumer Lending Group had suffered a breakdown of internal controls since 2005. Despite the findings of the investigation, Bowen's charges were ignored, even though withholding such information from shareholders violated the <a href="/wiki/Sarbanes%E2%80%93Oxley_Act" title="Sarbanes–Oxley Act">Sarbanes–Oxley Act</a> (SOX), which he had pointed out. Citigroup CEO <a href="/wiki/Charles_Prince" title="Charles Prince">Charles Prince</a> signed a certification that the bank was in compliance with SOX despite Bowen revealing this wasn't so. Citigroup eventually stripped Bowen of most of his responsibilities and informed him that his physical presence was no longer required at the bank. The Financial Crisis Inquiry Commission asked him to testify about Citigroup's role in the mortgage crisis, and he did so, appearing as one of the first witnesses before the Commission in April 2010.<sup id="cite_ref-Prosecuting_Wall_Street_85-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Prosecuting_Wall_Street-85">[85]</a></sup></p><p>As the crisis began to unfold, Citigroup announced on April 11, 2007, that it would eliminate 17,000 jobs, or about 5 percent of its workforce, in a broad restructuring designed to cut costs and bolster its long underperforming stock.<sup id="cite_ref-86" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-86">[86]</a></sup> Even after <a href="/wiki/Security_(finance)" title="Security (finance)">securities</a> and <a href="/wiki/Brokerage" class="mw-redirect" title="Brokerage">brokerage</a> firm <a href="/wiki/Bear_Stearns" title="Bear Stearns">Bear Stearns</a> ran into serious trouble in summer 2007, Citigroup decided the possibility of trouble with its CDOs was so tiny (less than 1/100 of 1%) that they excluded them from their risk analysis. With the crisis worsening, Citigroup announced on January 7, 2008 that it was considering cutting another 5 percent to 10 percent of its 327,000 member-workforce.<sup id="cite_ref-87" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-87">[87]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-jobcuts1_88-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-jobcuts1-88">[88]</a></sup></p>
<h3 class="in-block"><span id="Collapse_and_US_government_intervention_.282008.29"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Collapse_and_US_government_intervention_(2008)">Collapse and US government intervention (2008)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><a href="/w/index.php?title=Citigroup&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Collapse and US government intervention (2008)" data-section="9" class="mw-ui-icon mw-ui-icon-element mw-ui-icon-wikimedia-edit-base20 edit-page mw-ui-icon-flush-right">Edit</a></span></h3>
<p>By November 2008, Citigroup was insolvent, despite its receipt of $25 billion in taxpayer-funded federal <a href="/wiki/Troubled_Asset_Relief_Program" title="Troubled Asset Relief Program">Troubled Asset Relief Program</a> funds. On November 17, 2008, Citigroup announced plans for about 52,000 new job cuts, on top of 23,000 cuts already made during 2008 in a huge job cull resulting from four quarters of consecutive losses and reports that it was unlikely to be in profit again before 2010. The same day on <a href="/wiki/Wall_Street" title="Wall Street">Wall Street</a> markets responded, with shares falling and dropping the company's market capitalization to $6 billion, down from $300 billion two years prior.<sup id="cite_ref-stockcollapse_89-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-stockcollapse-89">[89]</a></sup> Eventually staff cuts totaled over 100,000 employees.<sup id="cite_ref-jobcuts2_90-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-jobcuts2-90">[90]</a></sup> Its stock market value dropped to $20.5 billion, down from $244 billion two years earlier.<sup id="cite_ref-stockcollapse_89-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-stockcollapse-89">[89]</a></sup> Shares of Citigroup common stock traded well below $1.00 on the <a href="/wiki/New_York_Stock_Exchange" title="New York Stock Exchange">New York Stock Exchange</a>.
</p><p>As a result, late in the evening on November 23, 2008, Citigroup and Federal regulators approved a plan to stabilize the company and forestall a further deterioration in the company's value. On November 24, 2008, the U.S. government announced a massive bailout for Citigroup designed to rescue the company from bankruptcy while giving the government a major say in its operations. A joint statement by the <a href="/wiki/US_Treasury_Department" class="mw-redirect" title="US Treasury Department">US Treasury Department</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Federal_Reserve" title="Federal Reserve">Federal Reserve</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Federal_Deposit_Insurance_Corp" class="mw-redirect" title="Federal Deposit Insurance Corp">Federal Deposit Insurance Corp</a> announced: "With these transactions, the U.S. government is taking the actions necessary to strengthen the financial system and protect U.S. taxpayers and the U.S. economy."<sup id="cite_ref-91" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-91">[91]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-92" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-92">[92]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-93" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-93">[93]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-94" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-94">[94]</a></sup> The bailout called for the government to back about $306 billion in loans and securities and directly invest about $20 billion in the company. The Treasury provided $20 billion in <a href="/wiki/Troubled_Asset_Relief_Program" title="Troubled Asset Relief Program">Troubled Asset Relief Program</a> (TARP) funds in addition to $25 billion given in October. The Treasury Department, the <a href="/wiki/Federal_Reserve" title="Federal Reserve">Federal Reserve</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Federal_Deposit_Insurance_Corporation" title="Federal Deposit Insurance Corporation">Federal Deposit Insurance Corporation</a> (FDIC) agreed to cover 90% of the losses on Citigroup's $335 billion portfolio after Citigroup absorbed the first $29 billion in losses. The Treasury would assume the first $5 billion in losses; the FDIC would absorb the next $10 billion; then the Federal Reserve would assume the rest of the risk. The assets remained on Citigroup's balance sheet; the technical term for this arrangement is <a href="/wiki/Ring_fence" class="mw-redirect" title="Ring fence">ring fencing</a>.
</p><p>In return the bank gave the U.S. Treasury $27 billion of preferred shares and warrants to acquire <a href="/wiki/Common_stock" title="Common stock">common stock</a>. The government obtained wide powers over banking operations. Citigroup agreed to try to modify mortgages, using standards set up by the FDIC after the collapse of <a href="/wiki/IndyMac_Bank" class="mw-redirect" title="IndyMac Bank">IndyMac Bank</a>, with the goal of keeping as many homeowners as possible in their houses. Executive salaries would be capped.<sup id="cite_ref-95" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-95">[95]</a></sup> As a condition of the federal assistance, Citigroup's dividend payment was reduced to $0.01 per share.
</p><p>According to <i><a href="/wiki/The_Wall_Street_Journal" title="The Wall Street Journal">The Wall Street Journal</a></i>, the government aid provided to Citi in 2008/2009 was provided to prevent a worldwide chaos and panic by the potential collapse of its Global Transactions Services (now TTS) division. According to the article, former CEO Pandit said if Citigroup was allowed to unravel into bankruptcy, "100 governments around the world would be trying to figure out how to pay their employees".<sup id="cite_ref-96" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-96">[96]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-97" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-97">[97]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-98" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-98">[98]</a></sup></p><p>In 2009, <a href="/wiki/Jane_Fraser_(banking)" title="Jane Fraser (banking)">Jane Fraser</a>, the CEO of Citi Private Bank, stopped paying its bankers with commission for selling investment products, in a move to bolster Citi Private Bank's reputation as an independent wealth management adviser, as opposed to a product pusher.<sup id="cite_ref-99" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-99">[99]</a></sup></p>
<h3 class="in-block"><span id="Creation_of_Citi_Holdings_.282009.29"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Creation_of_Citi_Holdings_(2009)">Creation of Citi Holdings (2009)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><a href="/w/index.php?title=Citigroup&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Creation of Citi Holdings (2009)" data-section="10" class="mw-ui-icon mw-ui-icon-element mw-ui-icon-wikimedia-edit-base20 edit-page mw-ui-icon-flush-right">Edit</a></span></h3>
<p>On January 16, 2009, Citigroup announced its intention to reorganize itself into two operating units: Citicorp for its retail and institutional client business, and Citi Holdings for its brokerage and asset management.<sup id="cite_ref-prsplit_100-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-prsplit-100">[100]</a></sup> Citigroup will continue to operate as a single company for the time being, but Citi Holdings managers will be tasked to "take advantage of value-enhancing disposition and combination opportunities as they emerge",<sup id="cite_ref-prsplit_100-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-prsplit-100">[100]</a></sup> and eventual spin-offs or mergers involving either operating unit were not ruled out.<sup id="cite_ref-101" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-101">[101]</a></sup> Citi Holdings consists of Citi businesses that Citi wants to sell and are not considered part of Citi's core businesses. The majority of its assets are U.S. mortgages. It was created in the wake of the financial crisis as part of Citi's restructuring plan. It consists of several business entities including remaining interests in local consumer lending such as OneMain Financial, divestitures such as Smith Barney, and a special asset pool. Citi Holdings represents $156 billion of GAAP assets, or ~8% of Citigroup; 59% represents North American mortgages, 18% operating businesses, 13% special asset pool, and 10% categorized as other. Operating businesses include OneMain Financial ($10B), PrimeRe ($7B), MSSB JV ($8B) and Spain / Greece retail ($4B), less associated loan loss reserves. While Citi Holdings is a mixed bag, its primary objective is to wind down some non-core businesses and reduce assets, and strategically "breaking even" in 2015.<sup id="cite_ref-102" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-102">[102]</a></sup></p><p>On February 27, 2009, Citigroup announced that the U.S. government would take a 36% <a href="/wiki/Stock" title="Stock">equity</a> stake in the company by converting US$25 billion in emergency aid into <a href="/wiki/Common_stock" title="Common stock">common stock</a> with a <a href="/wiki/United_States_Treasury" class="mw-redirect" title="United States Treasury">United States Treasury</a> credit line of $45 billion to prevent the bankruptcy of the company.<sup id="cite_ref-103" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-103">[103]</a></sup> The government guaranteed losses on more than $300 billion of troubled assets and injected $20 billion immediately into the company. The salary of the CEO was set at $1 per year and the highest salary of employees was restricted to $500,000. Any compensation amount above $500,000 had to be paid with <a href="/wiki/Restricted_stock" title="Restricted stock">restricted stock</a> that could not be sold by the employee until the emergency government aid was repaid in full.<sup id="cite_ref-104" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-104">[104]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-105" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-105">[105]</a></sup> The U.S. government also gained control of half the seats in the Board of Directors, and the senior management was subjected to removal by the US government if there were poor performance. By December 2009, the U.S. government stake was reduced from a 36% stake to a 27% stake, after Citigroup sold $21 billion of common shares and equity in the largest single share sale in U.S. history, surpassing Bank of America's $19 billion share sale 1 month prior. By December 2010, Citigroup repaid the emergency aid in full and the U.S. government had made a $12 billion profit on its investment in the company.<sup id="cite_ref-106" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-106">[106]</a></sup> Government restrictions on pay and oversight of the senior management were removed after the U.S. government sold its remaining 27% stake in December 2010.<sup id="cite_ref-107" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-107">[107]</a></sup></p><p>On June 1, 2009, it was announced that Citigroup would be removed from the <a href="/wiki/Dow_Jones_Industrial_Average" title="Dow Jones Industrial Average">Dow Jones Industrial Average</a> effective June 8, 2009, due to significant government ownership. Citigroup was replaced by Travelers Co.<sup id="cite_ref-108" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-108">[108]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-109" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-109">[109]</a></sup></p>
<h4 class="in-block"><span id="Sale_of_Smith_Barney_.282009.29"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Sale_of_Smith_Barney_(2009)">Sale of Smith Barney (2009)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><a href="/w/index.php?title=Citigroup&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Sale of Smith Barney (2009)" data-section="11" class="mw-ui-icon mw-ui-icon-element mw-ui-icon-wikimedia-edit-base20 edit-page mw-ui-icon-flush-right">Edit</a></span></h4>
<p>Smith Barney, Citi's global private wealth management unit, provided brokerage, investment banking and asset management services to corporations, governments and individuals around the world. With over 800 offices worldwide, Smith Barney held 9.6 million domestic client accounts, representing $1.562 trillion in client assets worldwide.
</p><p>On January 13, 2009, Citi announced the merger of Smith Barney with <a href="/wiki/Morgan_Stanley_Wealth_Management" title="Morgan Stanley Wealth Management">Morgan Stanley Wealth Management</a>. Citi received $2.7 billion and a 49% interest in the joint venture.<sup id="cite_ref-110" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-110">[110]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-111" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-111">[111]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-112" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-112">[112]</a></sup></p><p>In June 2013, Citi sold its remaining 49% stake in Smith Barney to Morgan Stanley Wealth Management for $13.5 billion following an appraisal by Perella Weinberg.<sup id="cite_ref-113" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-113">[113]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-114" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-114">[114]</a></sup></p>
<h3 class="in-block"><span id="Return_to_profitability.2C_denationalization_.282010.29"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Return_to_profitability,_denationalization_(2010)">Return to profitability, denationalization (2010)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><a href="/w/index.php?title=Citigroup&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Return to profitability, denationalization (2010)" data-section="12" class="mw-ui-icon mw-ui-icon-element mw-ui-icon-wikimedia-edit-base20 edit-page mw-ui-icon-flush-right">Edit</a></span></h3>
<p>In 2010, Citigroup achieved its first profitable year since 2007. It reported $10.6 billion in net profit, compared with a $1.6 billion loss in 2009.<sup id="cite_ref-115" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-115">[115]</a></sup> Late in 2010, the government sold its remaining stock holding in the company, yielding an overall net profit to taxpayers of $12 billion.<sup id="cite_ref-116" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-116">[116]</a></sup> A special IRS tax exception given to Citi allowed the US Treasury to sell its shares at a profit, while it still owned Citigroup shares, which eventually netted $12 billion. According to Treasury spokeswoman Nayyera Haq, "This (IRS tax) rule was designed to stop corporate raiders from using loss corporations to evade taxes, and was never intended to address the unprecedented situation where the government owned shares in banks. And it was certainly not written to prevent the government from selling its shares for a profit."<sup id="cite_ref-117" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-117">[117]</a></sup></p>
<h3 class="in-block"><span id="Expansion_of_retail_banking_operations_.282011.29"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Expansion_of_retail_banking_operations_(2011)">Expansion of retail banking operations (2011)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><a href="/w/index.php?title=Citigroup&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Expansion of retail banking operations (2011)" data-section="13" class="mw-ui-icon mw-ui-icon-element mw-ui-icon-wikimedia-edit-base20 edit-page mw-ui-icon-flush-right">Edit</a></span></h3>
<p>In 2011, Citi was the first bank to introduce digitized Smart Banking branches in Washington, D.C., New York, Tokyo and Busan (South Korea) while it continued renovating its entire branch network.<sup id="cite_ref-118" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-118">[118]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-119" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-119">[119]</a></sup> New sales and service centers were also opened in Moscow and St. Petersburg. Citi Express modules, 24-hour service units, were introduced in Colombia. Citi opened additional branches in China, expanding its branch presence to 13 cities in China.<sup id="cite_ref-120" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-120">[120]</a></sup></p>
<h3 class="in-block"><span id="Expansion_of_credit_card_operations_.282011.29"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Expansion_of_credit_card_operations_(2011)">Expansion of credit card operations (2011)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><a href="/w/index.php?title=Citigroup&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Expansion of credit card operations (2011)" data-section="14" class="mw-ui-icon mw-ui-icon-element mw-ui-icon-wikimedia-edit-base20 edit-page mw-ui-icon-flush-right">Edit</a></span></h3>
<p>Citi Branded Cards introduced several new products in 2011, including: Citi ThankYou, Citi Executive/<a href="/wiki/AAdvantage" title="AAdvantage">AAdvantage</a> and Citi Simplicity cards in the U.S. It also has Latin America partnership cards with Colombia-based airline Avianca and with Banamex and AeroMexico; and a merchant loyalty program in Europe. Citibank is also the first and currently the only international bank to be approved by Chinese regulators to issue credit cards under its own brand without cooperating with Chinese state-owned domestic banks.<sup id="cite_ref-121" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-121">[121]</a></sup></p>
<h3 class="in-block"><span id="Chinese_investment_banking_joint_venture_.282012.29"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Chinese_investment_banking_joint_venture_(2012)">Chinese investment banking joint venture (2012)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><a href="/w/index.php?title=Citigroup&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Chinese investment banking joint venture (2012)" data-section="15" class="mw-ui-icon mw-ui-icon-element mw-ui-icon-wikimedia-edit-base20 edit-page mw-ui-icon-flush-right">Edit</a></span></h3>
<p>In 2012, the Global Markets division and Orient Securities formed form <a href="/wiki/Citi_Orient_Securities" title="Citi Orient Securities">Citi Orient Securities</a>, a Shanghai-based equity and debt brokerage operating in the Chinese market.<sup id="cite_ref-122" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-122">[122]</a></sup></p>
<h3 class="in-block"><span id="Federal_Reserve_stress_tests_.282012.E2.80.932016.29"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Federal_Reserve_stress_tests_(2012–2016)">Federal Reserve stress tests (2012–2016)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><a href="/w/index.php?title=Citigroup&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Federal Reserve stress tests (2012–2016)" data-section="16" class="mw-ui-icon mw-ui-icon-element mw-ui-icon-wikimedia-edit-base20 edit-page mw-ui-icon-flush-right">Edit</a></span></h3>
<p>On March 13, 2012, the Federal Reserve reported Citigroup is one of the four financial institutions, out of 19 major banks, that failed its <a href="/wiki/Stress_test_(financial)" title="Stress test (financial)">stress tests</a>, designed to measure bank capital during a financial crisis. The 2012 stress tests determined whether banks could withstand a financial crisis that has unemployment at 13%, stock prices to be cut in half, and home prices decreased by 21%.<sup id="cite_ref-123" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-123">[123]</a></sup> Citi failed the Fed stress tests due to Citi's high capital return plan and its international loans, which were rated by the Fed to be at higher risk than its domestic American loans.<sup id="cite_ref-124" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-124">[124]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-125" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-125">[125]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-126" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-126">[126]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-127" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-127">[127]</a></sup> Citi received half of its revenues from its international businesses. In comparison, <a href="/wiki/Bank_Of_America" class="mw-redirect" title="Bank Of America">Bank Of America</a>, which passed the stress test and did not ask for a capital return to investors, received 78% of its revenue in the United States.<sup id="cite_ref-128" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-128">[128]</a></sup></p><p>By June 2012, the year of Citi's 200th anniversary, Citigroup had built up $420 billion in surplus <a href="/wiki/Cash_reserve" class="mw-redirect" title="Cash reserve">cash reserves</a> and government securities.<sup id="cite_ref-129" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-129">[129]</a></sup> As of March 31, 2012, Citi had a <a href="/wiki/Tier_1_capital" title="Tier 1 capital">Tier 1 capital</a> ratio of 12.4%.<sup id="cite_ref-130" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-130">[130]</a></sup> This was a result of selling more than $500 billion of its special assets placed in Citi Holdings, which were guaranteed from losses by the US Treasury while under federal majority ownership.<sup id="cite_ref-131" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-131">[131]</a></sup></p><p>In 2013, <a href="/wiki/Sanjiv_Das" title="Sanjiv Das">Sanjiv Das</a> was replaced as head of CitiMortgage with <a href="/wiki/Jane_Fraser_(banking)" title="Jane Fraser (banking)">Jane Fraser</a>, former head of Citi Private Bank.<sup id="cite_ref-132" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-132">[132]</a></sup></p><p>On March 26, 2014, the <a href="/wiki/Federal_Reserve_Board_of_Governors" title="Federal Reserve Board of Governors">Federal Reserve Board of Governors</a> reported that Citigroup was one of the 5 financial institutions that failed its stress tests. Unlike in the failed stress test in 2012, Citigroup failed on qualitative concerns that were unresolved despite regulatory warnings. The report specifically stated that Citigroup failed "to project revenues and losses under a stressful scenario for material parts of the firm's global operations and its ability to develop scenarios for its internal stress testing that adequately reflects its full range business activities and exposures."<sup id="cite_ref-133" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-133">[133]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-134" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-134">[134]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-135" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-135">[135]</a></sup></p><p>On March 11, 2015, Citi has passed its first CCAR test,<sup id="cite_ref-136" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-136">[136]</a></sup> allowing it to raise its dividend to 5 cent a share and unveiling a plan for a $7.8 billion  <a href="/wiki/Share_repurchase" title="Share repurchase">share repurchase</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-137" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-137">[137]</a></sup></p><p>In February 2016, the company was subject to a lawsuit as a result of the bankruptcy of a Mexican oil services firm.<sup id="cite_ref-138" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-138">[138]</a></sup></p><p>In April 2016, Citigroup announced that it would eliminate its <a href="/wiki/Bad_bank" title="Bad bank">bad bank</a>, Citi Holdings.<sup id="cite_ref-139" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-139">[139]</a></sup></p><p>On June 23, 2016, Federal Reserve handed Citi a passing grade on its stress test the second time in a row, giving permission to triple its dividend to 16 cent a share and approving an $8.6 billion stock repurchase program,<sup id="cite_ref-140" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-140">[140]</a></sup></p>
<h4 class="in-block"><span id="Spin-off_of_Napier_Park_Global_Capital_.282013.29"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Spin-off_of_Napier_Park_Global_Capital_(2013)">Spin-off of Napier Park Global Capital (2013)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><a href="/w/index.php?title=Citigroup&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Spin-off of Napier Park Global Capital (2013)" data-section="17" class="mw-ui-icon mw-ui-icon-element mw-ui-icon-wikimedia-edit-base20 edit-page mw-ui-icon-flush-right">Edit</a></span></h4>
<p>Citi Capital Advisors (CCA), formerly Citi Alternative Investments, was a <a href="/wiki/Hedge_fund" title="Hedge fund">hedge fund</a> that offered various investment strategies across multiple asset classes. To comply with the <a href="/wiki/Volcker_Rule" title="Volcker Rule">Volcker Rule</a>, which limits bank ownership in hedge funds to no more than 3%, Citi spun off its hedge fund unit in 2013 and gave a majority of the company to its managers.<sup id="cite_ref-141" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-141">[141]</a></sup> The spin-off of CCA created Napier Park Global Capital, a $6.8 billion hedge fund with more than 100 employees in New York and London and managed by Jim O’Brien and Jonathan Dorfman.<sup id="cite_ref-142" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-142">[142]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-143" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-143">[143]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-144" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-144">[144]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-145" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-145">[145]</a></sup></p>
<h4 class="in-block"><span id="Downsizing_of_consumer_banking_unit_.282014.29"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Downsizing_of_consumer_banking_unit_(2014)">Downsizing of consumer banking unit (2014)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><a href="/w/index.php?title=Citigroup&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Downsizing of consumer banking unit (2014)" data-section="18" class="mw-ui-icon mw-ui-icon-element mw-ui-icon-wikimedia-edit-base20 edit-page mw-ui-icon-flush-right">Edit</a></span></h4>
<p>In October 2014, Citigroup announced its exit from consumer banking in 11 markets, including Costa Rica, El Salvador, Guatemala, Nicaragua, Panama, Peru, Japan, Guam, the Czech Republic, Egypt, South Korea (consumer finance only), and Hungary.<sup id="cite_ref-146" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-146">[146]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-147" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-147">[147]</a></sup></p>
<h3 class="in-block"><span class="mw-headline" id="2015_onwards">2015 onwards</span><span class="mw-editsection"><a href="/w/index.php?title=Citigroup&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: 2015 onwards" data-section="19" class="mw-ui-icon mw-ui-icon-element mw-ui-icon-wikimedia-edit-base20 edit-page mw-ui-icon-flush-right">Edit</a></span></h3>
<p>In May 2015, the bank announced the sale of its margin foreign exchange business, including <a href="/wiki/CitiFX_Pro" title="CitiFX Pro">CitiFX Pro</a> and TradeStream, to <a href="/wiki/FXCM" title="FXCM">FXCM</a> and  <a href="/wiki/SAXO_Bank" class="mw-redirect" title="SAXO Bank">SAXO Bank</a> of <a href="/wiki/Denmark" title="Denmark">Denmark</a>. Despite this deal, industry surveys pegged Citi as the biggest banking player in the forex market. The company's remaining foreign exchange sales &amp; trading businesses continued operating in the wake of this deal under the leadership of James Bindler, who succeeded Jeff Feig as the firm's global head of foreign exchange in 2014.<sup id="cite_ref-148" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-148">[148]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-149" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-149">[149]</a></sup></p><p>In February 2016, Citi sold its retail and commercial banking operations in Panama and Costa Rica to the <a href="/wiki/Bank_of_Nova_Scotia" class="mw-redirect" title="Bank of Nova Scotia">Bank of Nova Scotia</a> (Scotiabank) for $360 million. The operations sold include 27 branches serving approximately 250,000 clients. Citi continues to offer corporate and institutional banking and wealth management in Panama and Costa Rica.<sup id="cite_ref-150" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-150">[150]</a></sup></p><p>In November 2015, <a href="/wiki/Springleaf" class="mw-redirect" title="Springleaf">Springleaf</a> acquired <a href="/wiki/OneMain_Financial" title="OneMain Financial">OneMain Financial</a> from Citigroup.<sup id="cite_ref-151" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-151">[151]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-152" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-152">[152]</a></sup></p><p>On April 1, 2016, Citigroup became the exclusive issuer of Costco-branded credit cards.<sup id="cite_ref-153" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-153">[153]</a></sup></p><p>In April 2016, Citi was given regulatory approval for its 'living will,' its plans to shut down operations in the event of another financial crisis.<sup id="cite_ref-154" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-154">[154]</a></sup></p><p>According to data compiled by <a href="/wiki/Bloomberg_L.P." title="Bloomberg L.P.">Bloomberg</a>, Citigroup is ranked No. 10 in advising on mergers in Europe, the Middle East and Africa for the year 2018, while in America is ranked No.3.<sup id="cite_ref-155" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-155">[155]</a></sup></p><p>In response to the <a href="/wiki/2019%E2%80%9320_coronavirus_pandemic" title="2019–20 coronavirus pandemic">2019–20 coronavirus pandemic</a>, Citi provided support to cardholders including waiving late fees and so forth.<sup id="cite_ref-156" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-156">[156]</a></sup> It also announced that some lower paid employees would receive a one off payment of US$1,000 to help them through the crisis.<sup id="cite_ref-157" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-157">[157]</a></sup> This was not just limited to the US. In Singapore where Citi had a large operation, low paid staff would receive S$1,200.<sup id="cite_ref-158" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-158">[158]</a></sup></p>
</section><h2 class="section-heading" onclick="javascript:mfTempOpenSection(3)"><div class="mw-ui-icon mw-ui-icon-element indicator mw-ui-icon-small mw-ui-icon-flush-left"></div><span class="mw-headline" id="Offices">Offices</span><span class="mw-editsection"><a href="/w/index.php?title=Citigroup&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: Offices" data-section="20" class="mw-ui-icon mw-ui-icon-element mw-ui-icon-wikimedia-edit-base20 edit-page mw-ui-icon-flush-right">Edit</a></span></h2><section class="mf-section-3 collapsible-block" id="mf-section-3">
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Citigroup.canary.wharf.arp.500pix.jpg" class="image"><noscript><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c6/Citigroup.canary.wharf.arp.500pix.jpg/220px-Citigroup.canary.wharf.arp.500pix.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="294" class="thumbimage" data-file-width="500" data-file-height="668"></noscript><span class="lazy-image-placeholder" style="width: 220px;height: 294px;" data-src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c6/Citigroup.canary.wharf.arp.500pix.jpg/220px-Citigroup.canary.wharf.arp.500pix.jpg" data-alt="" data-width="220" data-height="294" data-class="thumbimage"> </span></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Citigroup.canary.wharf.arp.500pix.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Citigroup EMEA headquarters at the <a href="/wiki/Citigroup_Centre,_London" title="Citigroup Centre, London">Citigroup Centre, London</a>, <a href="/wiki/Canary_Wharf" title="Canary Wharf">Canary Wharf</a>, London</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Citigroup_Centre.jpg" class="image"><noscript><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4f/Citigroup_Centre.jpg/220px-Citigroup_Centre.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="363" class="thumbimage" data-file-width="515" data-file-height="849"></noscript><span class="lazy-image-placeholder" style="width: 220px;height: 363px;" data-src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4f/Citigroup_Centre.jpg/220px-Citigroup_Centre.jpg" data-alt="" data-width="220" data-height="363" data-class="thumbimage"> </span></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Citigroup_Centre.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Citigroup_Centre,_Sydney" title="Citigroup Centre, Sydney">Citigroup Centre, Sydney</a></div></div></div>
<h3 class="in-block"><span class="mw-headline" id="New_York_City">New York City</span><span class="mw-editsection"><a href="/w/index.php?title=Citigroup&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: New York City" data-section="21" class="mw-ui-icon mw-ui-icon-element mw-ui-icon-wikimedia-edit-base20 edit-page mw-ui-icon-flush-right">Edit</a></span></h3>
<p><a href="/wiki/Citigroup_Center" title="Citigroup Center">Citigroup Center</a>, a diagonal-roof skyscraper located in <a href="/wiki/Midtown_Manhattan" title="Midtown Manhattan">Midtown Manhattan</a>, New York City, is Citigroup's most famous office building, which despite popular belief is not the company's headquarters building.
