Citigroup has its headquarters located in downtown Tribeca (388 Greenwich).<sup id="cite_ref-159" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-159">[159]</a></sup> Citigroup also owns a building in <a href="/wiki/Tribeca,_Manhattan" class="mw-redirect" title="Tribeca, Manhattan">Tribeca, Manhattan</a> at <a href="/wiki/388_Greenwich_Street" title="388 Greenwich Street">388 Greenwich Street</a> that serves as headquarters for its Investment and Corporate Banking operations and was the former headquarters of the Travelers Group.<sup id="cite_ref-160" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-160">[160]</a></sup></p><p>All of Citigroup's New York City real estate, excluding the company's Smith Barney division and Wall Street trading division, lies along the <a href="/wiki/New_York_City_Subway" title="New York City Subway">New York City Subway</a>'s <a href="/wiki/IND_Queens_Boulevard_Line" title="IND Queens Boulevard Line">IND Queens Boulevard Line</a>, served by the <span class="nowrap"><a href="/wiki/E_(New_York_City_Subway_service)" title="E (New York City Subway service)">E</a></span> and ​<span class="nowrap"><a href="/wiki/M_(New_York_City_Subway_service)" title="M (New York City Subway service)">M</a></span> trains. Consequently, the company's Midtown buildings—including 787 Seventh Avenue, 666 Fifth Avenue, 399 Park Avenue, 485 Lexington, 153 East 53rd Street (Citigroup Center) in Manhattan, and <a href="/wiki/One_Court_Square" title="One Court Square">One Court Square</a> in <a href="/wiki/Long_Island_City" title="Long Island City">Long Island City</a>, <a href="/wiki/Queens" title="Queens">Queens</a>, are all on the short four-stop corridor of the Queens Boulevard Line between <a href="/wiki/Court_Square_(IND_Queens_Boulevard_Line)" class="mw-redirect" title="Court Square (IND Queens Boulevard Line)">Court Square</a> and <a href="/wiki/Seventh_Avenue_(IND_Queens_Boulevard_Line)" class="mw-redirect" title="Seventh Avenue (IND Queens Boulevard Line)">Seventh Avenue</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-161" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-161">[161]</a></sup></p>
<h3 class="in-block"><span class="mw-headline" id="Naming_rights_to_Citi_Field">Naming rights to Citi Field</span><span class="mw-editsection"><a href="/w/index.php?title=Citigroup&amp;action=edit&amp;section=22" title="Edit section: Naming rights to Citi Field" data-section="22" class="mw-ui-icon mw-ui-icon-element mw-ui-icon-wikimedia-edit-base20 edit-page mw-ui-icon-flush-right">Edit</a></span></h3>
<p>Citigroup owns the naming rights to <a href="/wiki/Citi_Field" title="Citi Field">Citi Field</a>, the home ballpark of the <a href="/wiki/New_York_Mets" title="New York Mets">New York Mets</a> <a href="/wiki/Major_League_Baseball" title="Major League Baseball">Major League Baseball</a> team, via a $400 million, 20-year deal that commenced with the stadium opening in 2009.<sup id="cite_ref-162" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-162">[162]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-163" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-163">[163]</a></sup></p>
<h3 class="in-block"><span class="mw-headline" id="Chicago">Chicago</span><span class="mw-editsection"><a href="/w/index.php?title=Citigroup&amp;action=edit&amp;section=23" title="Edit section: Chicago" data-section="23" class="mw-ui-icon mw-ui-icon-element mw-ui-icon-wikimedia-edit-base20 edit-page mw-ui-icon-flush-right">Edit</a></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:CitigroupCenterChicago.jpg" class="image"><noscript><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9b/CitigroupCenterChicago.jpg/220px-CitigroupCenterChicago.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="293" class="thumbimage" data-file-width="2304" data-file-height="3072"></noscript><span class="lazy-image-placeholder" style="width: 220px;height: 293px;" data-src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9b/CitigroupCenterChicago.jpg/220px-CitigroupCenterChicago.jpg" data-alt="" data-width="220" data-height="293" data-class="thumbimage"> </span></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:CitigroupCenterChicago.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Citigroup_Center_(Chicago)" class="mw-redirect" title="Citigroup Center (Chicago)">Citigroup Center, Chicago</a></div></div></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Citicorp_Center_(Chicago)" class="mw-redirect" title="Citicorp Center (Chicago)">Citicorp Center</a> in Chicago has a series of curved archways at its peak, and sits across the street from major competitor <a href="/wiki/ABN_AMRO" title="ABN AMRO">ABN AMRO</a>'s <a href="/wiki/ABN_AMRO_Plaza" class="mw-redirect" title="ABN AMRO Plaza">ABN AMRO Plaza</a>. It has shops and restaurants serving <a href="/wiki/Metra" title="Metra">Metra</a> customers via the <a href="/wiki/Ogilvie_Transportation_Center" title="Ogilvie Transportation Center">Ogilvie Transportation Center</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-164" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-164">[164]</a></sup></p>
<h3 class="in-block"><span class="mw-headline" id="Sioux_Falls">Sioux Falls</span><span class="mw-editsection"><a href="/w/index.php?title=Citigroup&amp;action=edit&amp;section=24" title="Edit section: Sioux Falls" data-section="24" class="mw-ui-icon mw-ui-icon-element mw-ui-icon-wikimedia-edit-base20 edit-page mw-ui-icon-flush-right">Edit</a></span></h3>
<p>Citibank moved its credit card operations to <a href="/wiki/Sioux_Falls" class="mw-redirect" title="Sioux Falls">Sioux Falls</a>, <a href="/wiki/South_Dakota" title="South Dakota">South Dakota</a> in 1981 after the state eliminated caps on interest rates.<sup id="cite_ref-165" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-165">[165]</a></sup> In 2013, Citibank employed 2,900 in Sioux Falls and is one of the largest employers in the city. In 2018, they began building a new headquarters that will be 150,000 square feet of office space on a 19-acre campus.<sup id="cite_ref-166" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-166">[166]</a></sup></p>
</section><h2 class="section-heading" onclick="javascript:mfTempOpenSection(4)"><div class="mw-ui-icon mw-ui-icon-element indicator mw-ui-icon-small mw-ui-icon-flush-left"></div><span id="Regulatory_action.2C_lawsuits.2C_and_arbitration"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Regulatory_action,_lawsuits,_and_arbitration">Regulatory action, lawsuits, and arbitration</span><span class="mw-editsection"><a href="/w/index.php?title=Citigroup&amp;action=edit&amp;section=25" title="Edit section: Regulatory action, lawsuits, and arbitration" data-section="25" class="mw-ui-icon mw-ui-icon-element mw-ui-icon-wikimedia-edit-base20 edit-page mw-ui-icon-flush-right">Edit</a></span></h2><section class="mf-section-4 collapsible-block" id="mf-section-4">
<p>In 2004, Japanese regulators took action against Citibank Japan loaning to a customer involved in stock manipulation. The regulator suspended bank activities in one branch and three offices, and restricted their consumer banking division. In 2009, Japanese regulators again took action against Citibank Japan, because the bank had not set up an effective <a href="/wiki/Money_laundering" title="Money laundering">money laundering</a> monitoring system. The regulators suspended sales operations within Citibank's retail banking for a month.<sup id="cite_ref-167" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-167">[167]</a></sup></p><p>On March 23, 2005, the National Association of Securities Dealers, the former name of the American self-regulatory organization for broker-dealers, now known as the <a href="/wiki/Financial_Industry_Regulatory_Authority" title="Financial Industry Regulatory Authority">Financial Industry Regulatory Authority</a> (FInRA) announced total fines of $21.25 million against Citigroup Global Markets, Inc., American Express Financial Advisors and Chase Investment Services regarding suitability and supervisory violations of their mutual fund sales practices between January 2002 and July 2003. The case against Citigroup involved recommendations and sales of Class B and Class C shares of mutual funds.<sup id="cite_ref-168" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-168">[168]</a></sup></p><p>On June 6, 2007, FInRA announced more than $15 million in fines and restitution against Citigroup Global Markets, Inc., to settle charges related to misleading documents and inadequate disclosure in retirement seminars and meetings for BellSouth Corp. employees in North Carolina and South Carolina. FInRA found that Citigroup did not properly supervise a team of brokers located in Charlotte, N.C., who used misleading sales materials during dozens of seminars and meetings for hundreds of BellSouth employees.<sup id="cite_ref-169" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-169">[169]</a></sup></p><p>In July 2010, Citigroup agreed to pay $75 million to settle civil charges that it misled investors over potential losses from high-risk mortgages. The <a href="/wiki/U.S._Securities_and_Exchange_Commission" title="U.S. Securities and Exchange Commission">U.S. Securities and Exchange Commission</a> said that Citigroup had made misleading statements about the company's exposure to subprime mortgages. In 2007, Citigroup indicated that its exposure was less than $13 billion, when in fact it was over $50 billion.<sup id="cite_ref-170" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-170">[170]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-171" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-171">[171]</a></sup></p><p>In April 2011, an arbitration panel ordered Citigroup Inc to pay $54.1 million for losses from municipal securities funds that cratered between 2007 and 2008.<sup id="cite_ref-172" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-172">[172]</a></sup></p><p>In August 2012, Citigroup agreed to pay almost $25 million to settle an investor lawsuit alleging the bank misled investors about the nature of mortgage-backed securities. The lawsuit was on behalf of investors who purchased certificates in one of two mortgage-backed securities trusts from Citigroup Mortgage Loan Trust Inc in 2007.<sup id="cite_ref-173" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-173">[173]</a></sup></p><p>In February 2012, Citigroup agreed to pay $158.3 million to settle claims that it falsely certified the quality of loans issued by its CitiMortgage unit over a period of more than six years, so that they would qualify for insurance from the <a href="/wiki/Federal_Housing_Administration" title="Federal Housing Administration">Federal Housing Administration</a>. The lawsuit was initially brought by Sherry Hunt, a CitiMortgage employee.<sup id="cite_ref-174" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-174">[174]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-175" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-175">[175]</a></sup></p><p>On February 9, 2012, it was announced that the five largest mortgage servicers (Ally/GMAC, Bank of America, Citi, JPMorgan Chase, and Wells Fargo) agreed to a historic settlement with the federal government and 49 states.<sup id="cite_ref-176" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-176">[176]</a></sup> The settlement, known as the National Mortgage Settlement (NMS), required the servicers to provide about $26 billion in relief to distressed homeowners and in direct payments to the states and federal government. This settlement amount makes the NMS the second largest civil settlement in U.S. history, only trailing the <a href="/wiki/Tobacco_Master_Settlement_Agreement" title="Tobacco Master Settlement Agreement">Tobacco Master Settlement Agreement</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-177" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-177">[177]</a></sup> The five banks were also required to comply with 305 new mortgage servicing standards. Oklahoma held out and agreed to settle with the banks separately.
</p><p>In 2014, Citigroup agreed to pay $7 billion to resolve claims it misled investors about shoddy mortgage-backed securities in the run-up to the financial crisis. Attorney General Eric H. Holder Jr. said "The bank’s misconduct was egregious. [...] As a result of their assurances that toxic financial products were sound, Citigroup was able to expand its market share and increase profits" and that "the settlement did not absolve the bank or its employees from facing criminal charges."<sup id="cite_ref-178" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-178">[178]</a></sup></p><p>In July 2015, Citigroup was fined $70 million by the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Consumer_Financial_Protection_Bureau" class="mw-redirect" title="United States Consumer Financial Protection Bureau">United States Consumer Financial Protection Bureau</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Office_of_the_Comptroller_of_the_Currency" title="Office of the Comptroller of the Currency">Office of the Comptroller of the Currency</a>, and ordered to pay $700 million to customers. Citigroup had conducted illegal practices in marketing add-on products for credit cards, including credit monitoring, debt-protection products and wallet-protection services.<sup id="cite_ref-179" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-179">[179]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-180" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-180">[180]</a></sup></p><p>In January 2017, Citigroup Global Markets Inc. was fined $25 million by the <a href="/wiki/Commodity_Futures_Trading_Commission" title="Commodity Futures Trading Commission">Commodity Futures Trading Commission</a> for <a href="/wiki/Spoofing_(finance)" title="Spoofing (finance)">order spoofing</a> in U.S. Treasury futures markets, i.e., placing orders that were intended to be cancelled before execution, and for failing to diligently supervise its employees with regard to spoofing.<sup id="cite_ref-181" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-181">[181]</a></sup></p>
<h3 class="in-block"><span id="Enron.2C_WorldCom.2C_and_Global_Crossing_bankruptcies"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Enron,_WorldCom,_and_Global_Crossing_bankruptcies">Enron, WorldCom, and Global Crossing bankruptcies</span><span class="mw-editsection"><a href="/w/index.php?title=Citigroup&amp;action=edit&amp;section=26" title="Edit section: Enron, WorldCom, and Global Crossing bankruptcies" data-section="26" class="mw-ui-icon mw-ui-icon-element mw-ui-icon-wikimedia-edit-base20 edit-page mw-ui-icon-flush-right">Edit</a></span></h3>
<p>On October 22, 2001, Citigroup was sued for violating federal securities laws by misrepresenting Citigroup's <a href="/wiki/Enron" title="Enron">Enron</a>-related exposure in its 2001 Annual Report and elsewhere, and failing to disclose the true extent of Citigroup's legal liability arising out of its 'structured finance' deals with Enron.<sup id="cite_ref-182" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-182">[182]</a></sup> In 2003, Citigroup paid $145 million in fines and penalties to settle claims by the <a href="/wiki/Securities_and_Exchange_Commission" class="mw-redirect" title="Securities and Exchange Commission">Securities and Exchange Commission</a> and the Manhattan district attorney's office.<sup id="cite_ref-183" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-183">[183]</a></sup></p><p>In 2004, Citigroup paid $2.65 billion pre-tax, or $1.64 billion after-tax, to settle a lawsuit concerning its role in selling stocks and bonds for <a href="/wiki/WorldCom" class="mw-redirect" title="WorldCom">WorldCom</a>, the second largest telecommunications company in the world, which collapsed after an <a href="/wiki/Accounting_scandal" class="mw-redirect" title="Accounting scandal">accounting scandal</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-184" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-184">[184]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-185" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-185">[185]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-186" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-186">[186]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-187" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-187">[187]</a></sup></p><p>On February 5, 2002, Citigroup was sued for violating federal securities laws and misleading investors by issuing false information about <a href="/wiki/Global_Crossing" title="Global Crossing">Global Crossing</a>’s revenues and financial performance. In 2005, Citigroup paid $75 million to settle the lawsuit.<sup id="cite_ref-188" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-188">[188]</a></sup> Citigroup was accused of issuing exaggerated research reports and not disclosing conflicts of interest.<sup id="cite_ref-189" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-189">[189]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-190" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-190">[190]</a></sup></p><p>In 2005, Citigroup paid $2 billion to settle a lawsuit filed by investors in <a href="/wiki/Enron" title="Enron">Enron</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-191" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-191">[191]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-192" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-192">[192]</a></sup> In 2008, Citi also agreed to pay $1.66 billion to Enron creditors.<sup id="cite_ref-193" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-193">[193]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-194" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-194">[194]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-195" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-195">[195]</a></sup></p><p>On November 8, 2007, Citigroup was sued for financial misrepresentations and omissions of what amounted to more than two years of income and an entire line of business. In 2012, the company paid $590 million to settle the case.<sup id="cite_ref-196" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-196">[196]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-197" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-197">[197]</a></sup></p>
</section><h2 class="section-heading" onclick="javascript:mfTempOpenSection(5)"><div class="mw-ui-icon mw-ui-icon-element indicator mw-ui-icon-small mw-ui-icon-flush-left"></div><span class="mw-headline" id="CEO-to-worker_pay_ratio">CEO-to-worker pay ratio</span><span class="mw-editsection"><a href="/w/index.php?title=Citigroup&amp;action=edit&amp;section=27" title="Edit section: CEO-to-worker pay ratio" data-section="27" class="mw-ui-icon mw-ui-icon-element mw-ui-icon-wikimedia-edit-base20 edit-page mw-ui-icon-flush-right">Edit</a></span></h2><section class="mf-section-5 collapsible-block" id="mf-section-5">
<p>Pursuant to Section 953(b) of the <a href="/wiki/Dodd-Frank_Wall_Street_Reform_and_Consumer_Protection_Act" class="mw-redirect" title="Dodd-Frank Wall Street Reform and Consumer Protection Act">Dodd-Frank Wall Street Reform and Consumer Protection Act</a>, publicly traded companies are required to disclose (1) the median total annual compensation of all employees other than the CEO and (2) the ratio of the CEO’s annual total compensation to that of the median employee.<sup id="cite_ref-198" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-198">[198]</a></sup></p><p>Total 2018 compensation for <a href="/wiki/Michael_Corbat" title="Michael Corbat">Michael Corbat</a>, CEO, amounted to $24,195,749, and total compensation of the median employee was determined to be $49,766. The resulting pay ratio is estimated to be 486:1.<sup id="cite_ref-199" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-199">[199]</a></sup></p>
</section><h2 class="section-heading" onclick="javascript:mfTempOpenSection(6)"><div class="mw-ui-icon mw-ui-icon-element indicator mw-ui-icon-small mw-ui-icon-flush-left"></div><span class="mw-headline" id="Criticism">Criticism</span><span class="mw-editsection"><a href="/w/index.php?title=Citigroup&amp;action=edit&amp;section=28" title="Edit section: Criticism" data-section="28" class="mw-ui-icon mw-ui-icon-element mw-ui-icon-wikimedia-edit-base20 edit-page mw-ui-icon-flush-right">Edit</a></span></h2><section class="mf-section-6 collapsible-block" id="mf-section-6">
<h3 class="in-block"><span class="mw-headline" id="Alleged_money_laundering_by_Raul_Salinas">Alleged money laundering by Raul Salinas</span><span class="mw-editsection"><a href="/w/index.php?title=Citigroup&amp;action=edit&amp;section=29" title="Edit section: Alleged money laundering by Raul Salinas" data-section="29" class="mw-ui-icon mw-ui-icon-element mw-ui-icon-wikimedia-edit-base20 edit-page mw-ui-icon-flush-right">Edit</a></span></h3>
<p>In 1998, the <a href="/wiki/General_Accounting_Office" class="mw-redirect" title="General Accounting Office">General Accounting Office</a> issued a report critical of Citibank's handling of funds received from <a href="/wiki/Raul_Salinas_de_Gortari" class="mw-redirect" title="Raul Salinas de Gortari">Raul Salinas de Gortari</a>, brother of <a href="/wiki/Carlos_Salinas" class="mw-redirect" title="Carlos Salinas">Carlos Salinas</a>, the former president of Mexico. The report, titled "Raul Salinas, Citibank and Alleged Money Laundering," indicated that Citibank facilitated the transfer of millions of dollars through complex financial transactions that hid the funds' paper trail. The report indicated that Citibank took on Salinas as a client without making a thorough inquiry as to how he made his fortune, an omission that a Citibank official called a violation of the bank's "know your customer" policy.<sup id="cite_ref-200" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-200">[200]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-201" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-201">[201]</a></sup></p>
<h3 class="in-block"><span class="mw-headline" id="Conflicts_of_interest_on_investment_research">Conflicts of interest on investment research</span><span class="mw-editsection"><a href="/w/index.php?title=Citigroup&amp;action=edit&amp;section=30" title="Edit section: Conflicts of interest on investment research" data-section="30" class="mw-ui-icon mw-ui-icon-element mw-ui-icon-wikimedia-edit-base20 edit-page mw-ui-icon-flush-right">Edit</a></span></h3>
<p>In December 2002, Citigroup paid fines totaling $400 million, to states and the federal government as part of a settlement involving charges that ten banks, including Citigroup, deceived investors with biased research. The total settlement with the ten banks was $1.4 billion. The settlement required that the banks separate investment banking from research, and ban any allocation of IPO shares.<sup id="cite_ref-202" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-202">[202]</a></sup></p>
<h3 class="in-block"><span class="mw-headline" id="Citigroup_proprietary_government_bond_trading_scandal_of_2004">Citigroup proprietary government bond trading scandal of 2004</span><span class="mw-editsection"><a href="/w/index.php?title=Citigroup&amp;action=edit&amp;section=31" title="Edit section: Citigroup proprietary government bond trading scandal of 2004" data-section="31" class="mw-ui-icon mw-ui-icon-element mw-ui-icon-wikimedia-edit-base20 edit-page mw-ui-icon-flush-right">Edit</a></span></h3>
<p>Citigroup was criticized for disrupting the European <a href="/wiki/Bond_market" title="Bond market">bond market</a> by rapidly selling €11 billion worth of bonds on August 2, 2004 on the MTS Group trading platform, driving down the price, and then buying it back at cheaper prices.<sup id="cite_ref-203" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-203">[203]</a></sup></p>
<h3 class="in-block"><span class="mw-headline" id="Plutonomy_report">Plutonomy report</span><span class="mw-editsection"><a href="/w/index.php?title=Citigroup&amp;action=edit&amp;section=32" title="Edit section: Plutonomy report" data-section="32" class="mw-ui-icon mw-ui-icon-element mw-ui-icon-wikimedia-edit-base20 edit-page mw-ui-icon-flush-right">Edit</a></span></h3>
<p>In a leaked report for their investor clients from 2005, a team of global strategists at Citigroup wrote an analysis of the global distribution of income and wealth and what the implications for investment are. In the report, they state that rising global imbalances have transformed advanced Anglo-American economies into <a href="/wiki/Plutonomy" title="Plutonomy">plutonomies</a>, in which economic growth is both powered by and consumed by the wealthy. The authors cite data showing that the top 1% households in the US economy account for about 20% of the total income in 2000, which is roughly equal to the share of the bottom 60% of households put together. Moreover, in terms of wealth they demonstrate even more inequality such that "[T]he top 1% of households also account for 33% of net worth, greater than the bottom 90% of households put together. It gets better (or worse, depending on your political stripe) - the top 1% of households account for 40% of financial net worth, more than the bottom 95% of households put together."<sup id="cite_ref-kms2005_204-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-kms2005-204">[204]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:<span>3</span></sup>
As regards the current plutonomies (namely <a href="/wiki/United_States" title="United States">United States</a>, <a href="/wiki/United_Kingdom" title="United Kingdom">United Kingdom</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Canada" title="Canada">Canada</a>), they put forward six key drivers for plutonomy: "...an ongoing [bio-]technological revolution; capitalist-friendly governments and tax regimes; globalization that re-arranges global supply chains with mobile well-capitalized elites and immigrants; greater financial complexity and innovation; the rule of law, and patent protection are all well."<sup id="cite_ref-kms2005_204-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-kms2005-204">[204]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:<span>9</span></sup> Next, the authors show that in industrialized countries, plutonomy has led to a relationship between income concentration (plutonomy) and the household savings rates such that the latter tend to fall in plutonomies because of the spending thrift of the wealthy.<sup id="cite_ref-kms2005_204-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-kms2005-204">[204]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:<span>18</span></sup> In view of the fact "...the rich have been getting progressively richer over the last 30 years, saving less and spending more, the fortunes of companies that sell to the rich ought to have been good."<sup id="cite_ref-kms2005_204-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-kms2005-204">[204]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:<span>26</span></sup> Hence, the authors urge their investors to invest in equities of companies that cater to the wealthy (i.e. luxury industry).
While refraining from judging plutonomy as good or bad, the ultimate objective of their report is to examine how to make money of the observed savings and consumption imbalances.<sup id="cite_ref-kms2005_204-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-kms2005-204">[204]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:<span>21,25</span></sup> The report has also figured in Michael Moore's <i>Capitalism: A love Story</i> while his portrayal of the report has been criticized by the authors.<sup id="cite_ref-205" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-205">[205]</a></sup> Later reports by the same principal author mainly confirmed the previous findings.<sup id="cite_ref-206" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-206">[206]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-207" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-207">[207]</a></sup></p>
<h3 class="in-block"><span class="mw-headline" id="TARP_funding">TARP funding</span><span class="mw-editsection"><a href="/w/index.php?title=Citigroup&amp;action=edit&amp;section=33" title="Edit section: TARP funding" data-section="33" class="mw-ui-icon mw-ui-icon-element mw-ui-icon-wikimedia-edit-base20 edit-page mw-ui-icon-flush-right">Edit</a></span></h3>
<p>In a <i>The New York Times</i> op-ed, Michael Lewis and David Einhorn described the November 2008 $306 billion guarantee as "an undisguised gift" without any real crisis motivating it.<sup id="cite_ref-208" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-208">[208]</a></sup></p><p>According to New York Attorney General <a href="/wiki/Andrew_Cuomo" title="Andrew Cuomo">Andrew Cuomo</a>, Citigroup paid hundreds of millions of dollars in bonuses to more than 1,038 of its employees after it had received its $45 billion TARP funds in late 2008. This included 738 employees each receiving $1 million in bonuses, 176 employees each receiving $2 million bonuses, 124 each receiving $3 million in bonuses, and 143 each receiving bonuses of $4 million to more than $10 million.<sup id="cite_ref-209" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-209">[209]</a></sup> As a result of the criticism and the U.S. Government's majority holding of Citigroup's <a href="/wiki/Common_shares" class="mw-redirect" title="Common shares">common shares</a>, compensation and bonuses were restricted from February 2009 until December 2010.<sup id="cite_ref-210" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-210">[210]</a></sup></p>
<h3 class="in-block"><span class="mw-headline" id="Terra_Securities_scandal">Terra Securities scandal</span><span class="mw-editsection"><a href="/w/index.php?title=Citigroup&amp;action=edit&amp;section=34" title="Edit section: Terra Securities scandal" data-section="34" class="mw-ui-icon mw-ui-icon-element mw-ui-icon-wikimedia-edit-base20 edit-page mw-ui-icon-flush-right">Edit</a></span></h3>
<p>In November 2007 it became public that Citigroup was heavily involved in the <a href="/wiki/Terra_Securities_scandal" title="Terra Securities scandal">Terra Securities scandal</a>, which involved investments by eight municipalities of Norway in various hedge funds in the United States bond market.<sup id="cite_ref-211" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-211">[211]</a></sup> The funds were sold by <a href="/wiki/Terra_Securities" title="Terra Securities">Terra Securities</a> to the municipalities, while the products were delivered by Citigroup. Terra Securities ASA filed for bankruptcy November 28, 2007, the day after they received a letter from the <a href="/wiki/Financial_Supervisory_Authority_of_Norway" title="Financial Supervisory Authority of Norway">Financial Supervisory Authority of Norway</a> announcing withdrawal of permissions to operate. The letter stated, "The Supervisory Authority contends that Citigroup's presentation, as well as the presentation from Terra Securities ASA, appears insufficient and misleading because central elements like information about potential extra payments and the size of these are omitted."<sup id="cite_ref-212" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-212">[212]</a></sup></p>
<h3 class="in-block"><span class="mw-headline" id="Allegations_of_theft_from_customer_accounts">Allegations of theft from customer accounts</span><span class="mw-editsection"><a href="/w/index.php?title=Citigroup&amp;action=edit&amp;section=35" title="Edit section: Allegations of theft from customer accounts" data-section="35" class="mw-ui-icon mw-ui-icon-element mw-ui-icon-wikimedia-edit-base20 edit-page mw-ui-icon-flush-right">Edit</a></span></h3>
<p>In August 2008, Citigroup agreed to pay nearly $18 million in refunds and fines to settle accusations by California Attorney General <a href="/wiki/Jerry_Brown" title="Jerry Brown">Jerry Brown</a> that it wrongly took funds from the accounts of credit card customers. Citigroup paid $14 million of restitution to roughly 53,000 customers nationwide. A three-year investigation found that Citigroup from 1992 to 2003 used an improper computerized "sweep" feature to move positive balances from card accounts into the bank's general fund, without telling cardholders.<sup id="cite_ref-theft_213-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-theft-213">[213]</a></sup> Brown said that Citigroup "knowingly stole from its customers, mostly poor people and the recently deceased, when it designed and implemented the sweeps...When a whistleblower uncovered the scam and brought it to his superiors [in 2001], they buried the information and continued the illegal practice."<sup id="cite_ref-theft_213-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-theft-213">[213]</a></sup></p>
<h3 class="in-block"><span class="mw-headline" id="Potential_restructuring_and_liquidation_by_regulators">Potential restructuring and liquidation by regulators</span><span class="mw-editsection"><a href="/w/index.php?title=Citigroup&amp;action=edit&amp;section=36" title="Edit section: Potential restructuring and liquidation by regulators" data-section="36" class="mw-ui-icon mw-ui-icon-element mw-ui-icon-wikimedia-edit-base20 edit-page mw-ui-icon-flush-right">Edit</a></span></h3>
<p>In September 2011, former Wall Street reporter <a href="/wiki/Ron_Suskind" title="Ron Suskind">Ron Suskind</a> stated that Treasury Secretary <a href="/wiki/Timothy_Geithner" title="Timothy Geithner">Timothy Geithner</a> ignored a 2009 order from President <a href="/wiki/Barack_Obama" title="Barack Obama">Barack Obama</a> to break up Citigroup in an enormous restructuring and <a href="/wiki/Liquidation" title="Liquidation">liquidation</a> process.<sup id="cite_ref-suskind_214-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-suskind-214">[214]</a></sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citing_sources" title="Wikipedia:Citing sources"><span title="This citation requires a reference to the specific page or range of pages in which the material appears. (December 2014)">page needed</span></a></i>]</sup> According to Suskind, Obama wanted to restructure the bank into several leaner and smaller companies while Geithner was executing stress tests of American financial institutions.
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Robert_Kuttner" title="Robert Kuttner">Robert Kuttner</a> wrote in his 2010 book <i>A Presidency in Peril</i> that in spring 2009, Geithner and chief economic adviser Larry Summers believed that they could not seize, liquidate and break up Citigroup because they lacked the legal authority or the tools to do so. The Treasury Department denied Suskind's account in an e-mail to the media stating "This account is simply untrue. The directive given by the president in March 2009, was to develop a contingency plan for tough restructurings if the government ended up owning large shares of institutions at the conclusion of the stress tests that Secretary Geithner worked aggressively to put in place as part of the Administration's Financial Stability Plan. While Treasury began work on those contingency plans, there was fortunately never a need to put them in place."<sup id="cite_ref-215" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-215">[215]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-216" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-216">[216]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-217" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-217">[217]</a></sup></p>
<h3 class="in-block"><span class="mw-headline" id="Shareholder_rejection_of_executive_compensation_plan">Shareholder rejection of executive compensation plan</span><span class="mw-editsection"><a href="/w/index.php?title=Citigroup&amp;action=edit&amp;section=37" title="Edit section: Shareholder rejection of executive compensation plan" data-section="37" class="mw-ui-icon mw-ui-icon-element mw-ui-icon-wikimedia-edit-base20 edit-page mw-ui-icon-flush-right">Edit</a></span></h3>
<p>At Citi's 2012 annual shareholders' meeting on April 17, Citi's executive compensation package was rejected, with approximately 55% of the votes being against approval. The non-binding vote was required under the Dodd-Frank Act, which requires corporations to hold advisory shareholder votes on their executive compensation plans. Many shareholders expressed concerns about Citi's failed 2012 Fed stress test and lack of long-term performance-based metrics in its executive compensation plan. One of the largest and most activist of the shareholders voting no, the <a href="/wiki/California_Public_Employees%27_Retirement_System" class="mw-redirect" title="California Public Employees' Retirement System">California Public Employees' Retirement System</a>, stated Citi "has not anchored rewards to performance".<sup id="cite_ref-218" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-218">[218]</a></sup> A Citigroup shareholder filed a lawsuit days after the vote, claiming Citi executives breached their fiduciary duties.<sup id="cite_ref-219" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-219">[219]</a></sup> In response, <a href="/wiki/Richard_Parsons_(businessman)" title="Richard Parsons (businessman)">Richard Parsons</a>, former chairman of Citigroup, called the vote a "serious matter". A spokeswoman for Citi said "Citi's Board of Directors takes the shareholder vote seriously, and along with senior management will consult with representative shareholders to understand their concerns" and that the Compensation committee of the Board "will carefully consider their (shareholder) input as we move forward".<sup id="cite_ref-220" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-220">[220]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-221" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-221">[221]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-222" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-222">[222]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-223" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-223">[223]</a></sup></p>
<h3 class="in-block"><span class="mw-headline" id="Accusations_of_futures_market_manipulation">Accusations of futures market manipulation</span><span class="mw-editsection"><a href="/w/index.php?title=Citigroup&amp;action=edit&amp;section=38" title="Edit section: Accusations of futures market manipulation" data-section="38" class="mw-ui-icon mw-ui-icon-element mw-ui-icon-wikimedia-edit-base20 edit-page mw-ui-icon-flush-right">Edit</a></span></h3>
<p>In January 2017, bank regulators fined Citigroup $25 million on account of five traders from the bank having manipulated U.S. Treasury futures more than 2,500 times between July 2011 and December 2012. Citigroup was criticized for failing to adequately supervise its traders and for not having systems in place to detect spoofing, which involves entering fake orders designed to fool others into thinking prices are poised to rise or fall.<sup id="cite_ref-224" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-224">[224]</a></sup></p>
<h3 class="in-block"><span class="mw-headline" id="Criminal_cartel_charges_in_Australia">Criminal cartel charges in Australia</span><span class="mw-editsection"><a href="/w/index.php?title=Citigroup&amp;action=edit&amp;section=39" title="Edit section: Criminal cartel charges in Australia" data-section="39" class="mw-ui-icon mw-ui-icon-element mw-ui-icon-wikimedia-edit-base20 edit-page mw-ui-icon-flush-right">Edit</a></span></h3>
<p>On 1 June 2018, the Australian Competition and Consumer Commission (ACCC) announced that criminal cartel charges are expected to be laid by the Commonwealth Director of Public Prosecutions (CDPP) against ANZ Bank, its Group Treasurer Rick Moscati, along with Deutsche Bank, Citigroup and a number of individuals.<sup id="cite_ref-accc-01062018_225-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-accc-01062018-225">[225]</a></sup></p>
</section><h2 class="section-heading" onclick="javascript:mfTempOpenSection(7)"><div class="mw-ui-icon mw-ui-icon-element indicator mw-ui-icon-small mw-ui-icon-flush-left"></div><span class="mw-headline" id="Communications">Communications</span><span class="mw-editsection"><a href="/w/index.php?title=Citigroup&amp;action=edit&amp;section=40" title="Edit section: Communications" data-section="40" class="mw-ui-icon mw-ui-icon-element mw-ui-icon-wikimedia-edit-base20 edit-page mw-ui-icon-flush-right">Edit</a></span></h2><section class="mf-section-7 collapsible-block" id="mf-section-7">
<h3 class="in-block"><span class="mw-headline" id="Lobbying">Lobbying</span><span class="mw-editsection"><a href="/w/index.php?title=Citigroup&amp;action=edit&amp;section=41" title="Edit section: Lobbying" data-section="41" class="mw-ui-icon mw-ui-icon-element mw-ui-icon-wikimedia-edit-base20 edit-page mw-ui-icon-flush-right">Edit</a></span></h3>
<p>Between 1998 and 2014, Citigroup spent nearly $100 million lobbying the federal government.<sup id="cite_ref-226" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-226">[226]</a></sup> As of 2008, Citigroup was the 16th largest political campaign contributor in the US, out of all organizations, according to the <a href="/wiki/Center_for_Responsive_Politics" title="Center for Responsive Politics">Center for Responsive Politics</a>. From 1989 to 2006, members of the firm donated over $23,033,490, 49% of which went to Democrats and 51% of which went to Republicans.<sup id="cite_ref-227" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-227">[227]</a></sup>
Matthew Vadum, a senior editor at the conservative <a href="/wiki/Capital_Research_Center" title="Capital Research Center">Capital Research Center</a>, acknowledged these figures, but pointed out that Citigroup had been "a longtime donor to left-wing pressure groups", and referred to a Capital Research Center Foundation Watch 2006 study of Fortune 100 foundation giving, where Citigroup's foundation gave "20 times more money to groups on the left than to groups on the right" during tax year 2003.<sup id="cite_ref-228" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-228">[228]</a></sup></p><p>In 2014 Citigroup's PAC contributed $804,000 to campaigns of various members of Congress, i.e. 162 members of the House, including 72 Democrats, where donations averaged about $5,000 per candidate. Of the 57 Democrats supporting the 2015 Spending bill, 34 had received campaign cash from Citigroup's PAC at some point since 2010.<sup id="cite_ref-crp_229-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-crp-229">[229]</a></sup> Citigroup's 2014 donations favored Republicans only slightly. The bank's PAC had been nearly as generous to Democrats as Republicans – $30,000 to the <a href="/wiki/Democratic_Congressional_Campaign_Committee" title="Democratic Congressional Campaign Committee">Democratic Congressional Campaign Committee</a> (the maximum) and $10,000 to the 'New Democrat Coalition', a group of moderate Democrats most of whom voted for the 2015 spending package. Citibank's PAC made donations to both the campaigns and the leadership PACs of many top Democrats who voted for the 2015 spending bill, including <a href="/wiki/Steny_Hoyer" title="Steny Hoyer">Steny Hoyer</a> (Md.) <a href="/wiki/House_Democratic_Whip" class="mw-redirect" title="House Democratic Whip">House Democratic Whip</a> and Representatives <a href="/wiki/Jim_Himes" title="Jim Himes">Jim Himes</a> (D-Conn.) and <a href="/wiki/Debbie_Wasserman_Schultz" title="Debbie Wasserman Schultz">Debbie Wasserman Schultz</a> (D-Florida.).<sup id="cite_ref-crp_229-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-crp-229">[229]</a></sup></p>
<h3 class="in-block"><span class="mw-headline" id="Public_and_governmental_relations">Public and governmental relations</span><span class="mw-editsection"><a href="/w/index.php?title=Citigroup&amp;action=edit&amp;section=42" title="Edit section: Public and governmental relations" data-section="42" class="mw-ui-icon mw-ui-icon-element mw-ui-icon-wikimedia-edit-base20 edit-page mw-ui-icon-flush-right">Edit</a></span></h3>
<p>In 2009, former chairman Richard Parsons hired long-time Washington, D.C. lobbyist <a href="/wiki/Richard_F._Hohlt" title="Richard F. Hohlt">Richard F. Hohlt</a> to advise him and the company about relations with the U.S. government, though not to lobby for the company. While some speculated anonymously that the <a href="/wiki/Federal_Deposit_Insurance_Corporation" title="Federal Deposit Insurance Corporation">Federal Deposit Insurance Corporation</a> (FDIC) would have been a particular focus of Hohlt's attention, Hohlt said he'd had no contact with the government insurance corporation. Some former regulators found room to criticize Hohlt's involvement with Citigroup, because of his earlier involvement with the financial-services industry during the <a href="/wiki/Savings_and_loan_crisis" title="Savings and loan crisis">savings and loan crisis</a> of the 1980s. Hohlt responded that though mistakes were made in the earlier episode he'd never been investigated by any government agency and his experience gave him reason to be back in the "operating room" as parties address the more recent crisis.<sup id="cite_ref-230" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-230">[230]</a></sup></p><p>In 2010, the company named <a href="/wiki/Edward_Skyler" title="Edward Skyler">Edward Skyler</a>, formerly in New York City government and at <a href="/wiki/Bloomberg_L.P." title="Bloomberg L.P.">Bloomberg L.P.</a>, to its senior public and governmental relations position.<sup id="cite_ref-231" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-231">[231]</a></sup> Before Skyler was named and before he began his job search, the company reportedly held discussions with three other individuals to fill the position: NY Deputy Mayor <a href="/wiki/Kevin_Sheekey" title="Kevin Sheekey">Kevin Sheekey</a>, Mayor <a href="/wiki/Michael_Bloomberg" title="Michael Bloomberg">Michael Bloomberg</a>'s "political guru ... [who] spearheaded ... his short-lived flirtation with a presidential run ..., who will soon leave City Hall for a position at the mayor's company, Bloomberg L.P. .... After Mr. Bloomberg's improbable victory in the 2001 mayor's race, both Mr. Skyler and Mr. Sheekey followed him from his company to City Hall. Since then, they have been a part of an enormously influential coterie of advisers"; <a href="/wiki/Howard_Wolfson" title="Howard Wolfson">Howard Wolfson</a>, the former communications director for <a href="/wiki/Hillary_Clinton" title="Hillary Clinton">Hillary Clinton</a>'s presidential campaign and Mr. Bloomberg's re-election bid; and <a href="/wiki/Gary_Ginsberg" title="Gary Ginsberg">Gary Ginsberg</a>, now at <a href="/wiki/Time_Warner" class="mw-redirect" title="Time Warner">Time Warner</a> and formerly at <a href="/wiki/News_Corporation_(1980%E2%80%932013)" title="News Corporation (1980–2013)">News Corporation</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-NYT01_232-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NYT01-232">[232]</a></sup></p><p>On 21 March 2018, it was announced that Citigroup changed its policy to forbid its business customers from performing certain firearm related transactions. The policy doesn't affect clients who offer credit cards backed by Citigroup or borrow money, use banking services or raise capital through the company.<sup id="cite_ref-233" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-233">[233]</a></sup></p><p>One 19 March 2020, Citi announced that it would support 2019 coronavirus pandemic relief with some US$15 million<sup id="cite_ref-234" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-234">[234]</a></sup>. 
</p>
</section><h2 class="section-heading" onclick="javascript:mfTempOpenSection(8)"><div class="mw-ui-icon mw-ui-icon-element indicator mw-ui-icon-small mw-ui-icon-flush-left"></div><span class="mw-headline" id="Notable_staff">Notable staff</span><span class="mw-editsection"><a href="/w/index.php?title=Citigroup&amp;action=edit&amp;section=43" title="Edit section: Notable staff" data-section="43" class="mw-ui-icon mw-ui-icon-element mw-ui-icon-wikimedia-edit-base20 edit-page mw-ui-icon-flush-right">Edit</a></span></h2><section class="mf-section-8 collapsible-block" id="mf-section-8">
<h3 class="in-block"><span class="mw-headline" id="Current">Current</span><span class="mw-editsection"><a href="/w/index.php?title=Citigroup&amp;action=edit&amp;section=44" title="Edit section: Current" data-section="44" class="mw-ui-icon mw-ui-icon-element mw-ui-icon-wikimedia-edit-base20 edit-page mw-ui-icon-flush-right">Edit</a></span></h3>
<ul><li><a href="/wiki/Michael_Corbat" title="Michael Corbat">Michael Corbat</a> - current CEO starting from October 2012. Corbat stated that the large pay gap at the firm should inspire lower paid workers to take over his role.<sup id="cite_ref-235" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-235">[235]</a></sup> He received total compensation of US$24 million for 2019<sup id="cite_ref-236" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-236">[236]</a></sup>, which was in line with what many other banks such JP Morgan, Bank of America and Morgan Stanley CEOs for the same period<sup id="cite_ref-237" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-237">[237]</a></sup>.</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Jane_Fraser_(banking)" title="Jane Fraser (banking)">Jane Fraser</a> is a <a href="/wiki/Scottish_Americans" title="Scottish Americans">Scottish-American</a> banking executive. She currently holds the position of President of Citi, Chief Executive Officer, Global Consumer Banking<sup id="cite_ref-238" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-238">[238]</a></sup>. Educated at <a href="/wiki/Girton_College,_Cambridge" title="Girton College, Cambridge">Girton College, Cambridge</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Harvard_Business_School" title="Harvard Business School">Harvard Business School</a>, she was a partner at <a href="/wiki/McKinsey_%26_Company" title="McKinsey &amp; Company">McKinsey &amp; Company</a> for 10 years before joining <a class="mw-selflink selflink">Citigroup</a> in 2004. She has been promoted numerous times and acceded to four <a href="/wiki/Chief_executive_officer" title="Chief executive officer">CEO</a> posts, the latest being CEO of Citigroup Latin America in April 2015. She was included on <i><a href="/wiki/Fortune_(magazine)" title="Fortune (magazine)">Fortune</a></i>'s "Most Powerful Women in Business" list in 2014 and 2015 and has been called the "Number 1 Woman to Watch" for two consecutive years by <i><a href="/wiki/American_Banker" title="American Banker">American Banker</a></i>.</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Edward_Skyler" title="Edward Skyler">Edward Skyler</a> is an <a href="/wiki/United_States" title="United States">American</a> politician and businessperson. He was Deputy Mayor for Operations for <a href="/wiki/New_York_City" title="New York City">New York City</a>, the youngest deputy mayor in New York City's history. In 2010, he was named  Executive Vice President, Global Public Affairs at <a href="/wiki/Citigroup#Public_and_government_relations" title="Citigroup">Citigroup</a><sup id="cite_ref-239" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-239">[239]</a></sup>.</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Catherine_L._Mann" title="Catherine L. Mann">Catherine L. Mann</a> has been the current chief economist since 2018.</li></ul><h3 class="in-block"><span class="mw-headline" id="Former">Former</span><span class="mw-editsection"><a href="/w/index.php?title=Citigroup&amp;action=edit&amp;section=45" title="Edit section: Former" data-section="45" class="mw-ui-icon mw-ui-icon-element mw-ui-icon-wikimedia-edit-base20 edit-page mw-ui-icon-flush-right">Edit</a></span></h3>
<ul><li><a href="/wiki/Sanford_I._Weill" title="Sanford I. Weill">Sanford I. Weill</a> - was CEO from 1998 until October 1, 2003. He was also one of the 25 people that Time magazine blamed for the financial crisis<sup id="cite_ref-240" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-240">[240]</a></sup>.</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Charles_Prince" title="Charles Prince">Charles Prince</a> - was CEO from 2003 to November 2007. Prince was famously quoted as saying Citigroup was "still dancing" just as the financial crisis hit<sup id="cite_ref-241" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-241">[241]</a></sup>.</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Vikram_Pandit" title="Vikram Pandit">Vikram Pandit</a> - was CEO from December 2007 to October 2012.</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Willem_Buiter" title="Willem Buiter">Willem Buiter</a> - was chief economist from 2010 until 2018.</li></ul></section><h2 class="section-heading" onclick="javascript:mfTempOpenSection(9)"><div class="mw-ui-icon mw-ui-icon-element indicator mw-ui-icon-small mw-ui-icon-flush-left"></div><span class="mw-headline" id="References">References</span><span class="mw-editsection"><a href="/w/index.php?title=Citigroup&amp;action=edit&amp;section=46" title="Edit section: References" data-section="46" class="mw-ui-icon mw-ui-icon-element mw-ui-icon-wikimedia-edit-base20 edit-page mw-ui-icon-flush-right">Edit</a></span></h2><section class="mf-section-9 collapsible-block" id="mf-section-9">
